Attributes of Allah: Neither Identical Nor Other
States the consensus and explains the formula 'neither He nor other'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni consensus on the relationship between Allah's attributes and His essence, and why the formula 'neither He nor other than Him' avoids both identity and separation.

Core Idea
Attributes are conceptually distinct from the essence but existentially one with it.

This lesson presents a foundational Sunni doctrine on divine attributes. The early scholars (Companions, Followers, and later mujtahids) agreed on a precise formula to safeguard tawhid: every attribute of Allah is neither identical to His essence nor different from it. Understanding this formula is crucial for avoiding both anthropomorphism (tashbih) and denial of attributes (ta`til).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the consensus and the formula.
Pay attention to the distinction between mental concept and external existence.
2
Recognize the unanimous consensus
Note that the author cites the Companions, Followers, and mujtahids as having agreed on this formula. This ijma gives the doctrine authority.
3
Understand the two aspects: mental vs. external
The formula is explained in two dimensions: in mental concept (the attribute's meaning is distinct from the essence) and in external existence (the attribute is not a separate entity). Reflect on why both are necessary.
This avoids both saying the attribute is the essence (which would make 'knowledge' and 'power' meaningless) and saying it is other (which would imply multiplicity).
4
Reflect on the manifestation in creation
The passage ends by noting that attributes are not different from the essence in terms of their manifestation in existent things. Consider how this preserves divine unity while allowing multiple attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت خبير بأن المتحرك من بينهم فاعلم أن الصحابة والتابعين وغيرهم من المجتهدين رضوان الله تعالى عليهم أجمعين، قد أجمعوا على أن كل صفة من صفات الله تعالى لا هو ولا غيره، كذا ذكره الشارح؛ والمعنى أنها لا هو بحسب المفهوم الذهني، ولا غيره بحسب الوجود الخارجي، فإن مفهوم الصفات غير مفهوم الذات إلا أنها لا تغايرها باعتبار ظهورها في الكائنات.
And you are aware that the mover among them... so know that the Companions, the Followers, and other mujtahids—may Allah be pleased with them all—have unanimously agreed that every attribute of Allah is neither He nor other than Him. Thus the commentator mentioned it. The meaning is that it is not He in terms of mental concept, nor other than Him in terms of external existence. For the concept of the attributes is different from the concept of the essence, yet they are not different from it in terms of their manifestation in existent things.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni doctrine on the relationship between Allah's attributes and His essence. It begins by stating that the Companions and later mujtahids have a consensus (ijma') on this point. The famous formula is: 'every attribute of Allah is neither He nor other than Him' (لا هو ولا غيره). The author then explains this formula: in mental conception (al-mutawamm al-dhihnī), the meaning of each attribute (e.g., knowledge, power) is distinct from the meaning of the essence; we can conceptualize 'knowledge' without immediately thinking of 'essence'. However, in external existence (al-wujūd al-khārijī), the attribute is not a separate entity alongside the essence—it is the essence itself considered under a specific description. So the attributes are not identical in concept (because we distinguish them) nor different in external reality (because they are one being). The phrase 'باعتبار ظهورها في الكائنات' indicates that the multiplicity of attributes relates to the diverse manifestations of the divine essence as it relates to creation, not to any composition or multiplicity in the essence itself.

Key Terms
لا هو ولا غيره
المفهوم الذهني
الوجود الخارجي
الذات
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'neither identical nor other' is contradictory or means the attributes are both identical and different in the same sense.
Correction: The formula uses two different respects: mental concept (distinct) and external existence (one). There is no contradiction because the perspectives differ.

Checkpoint
What does the consensus 'لا هو ولا غيره' mean for each attribute of Allah?
A) The attribute is identical to the essence in every way
B) The attribute is a separate entity from the essence
C) The attribute is distinct in conception but not separate in external existence
D) The attribute is different from the essence only in the mind of the believer

Key Takeaway
The attributes of Allah are not identical to His essence in mental conception, but they are not separate entities in external existence; they are one with the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His attribute of Mercy, do you feel as though you are calling upon a separate quality He possesses, or do you realize that His Mercy is none other than His very Self reaching out to you? How would your certainty in His response change if you truly believed His attributes are not other than Him?
?
The lesson teaches that the attributes are distinct in our minds but one in reality. Is your worship fragmented—sometimes focusing on Allah's Power, sometimes on His Love—as if they were separate gods? How can you train your heart to see all attributes as the single reality of Allah, without losing their distinct meanings?

Why 'Al-Qadim' Is Not Among the Beautiful Names
Argues that al-Qadim is not a beautiful name with linguistic and shar'i evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the name 'al-Qadim' is not considered one of Allah's beautiful names according to this text, and how the names 'al-Awwal' and 'al-Qayyum' are more precise.

Core Idea
'Al-Qadim' is a theological term, not a revealed divine name; Allah's names are revealed.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle distinction between theological usage and revealed scripture. While scholars of kalam commonly refer to Allah as 'al-Qadim' (the Eternal), the author argues that this term is not one of the beautiful names (al-asmā' al-ḥusnā) revealed in the Qur'an and Sunnah. Instead, the revealed name 'al-Awwal' (the First) and 'al-Qayyum' (the Self-Subsistent) capture the intended meanings more precisely, one stressing temporal priority and the other eternal existence and self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the passage and locate the sentence where the author states that 'al-Qadim is not among the beautiful names.' Notice the contrast with the mutakallimūn who use it.
This claim is the heart of the lesson.
2
Examine the linguistic evidence
Look at the examples from the Quran (old date-stalk, ancient lie) that show 'qadim' in Arabic means 'prior in time' or 'old,' not 'without beginning.' Understand why this relativizes its meaning.
The author uses the word's usage in the Quran itself to argue that it does not denote absolute priority.
3
Compare 'al-Awwal' and 'al-Qayyum'
Read the final part where the author praises 'al-Awwal' and 'al-Qayyum' over 'al-Qadim.' Note how 'al-Awwal' implies everything after depends on Him, and 'al-Qayyum' adds self-subsistence and eternal duration.
This comparison shows the precision of revealed names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن كلامه وهو قديم بذاته وصفاته والقديمية مستلزمة للبقائية؛ لأن ما ثبت قدمه يستحيل عدمه، كما هو مستفاد من قوله تعالى: ﴿ هُوَ الْأَوَّلُ وَالْآخِرُ ﴾ [الحديد:3]، أي بلا ابتداء ولا انتهاء. وأما القديم فليس من الأسماء الحسنى وإن أطلقه عليه علماء الكلام مع أنه أنكره كثير السلف الكرام، وكذا بعض من الخلف الفخام، ومنهم ابن حزم ذهابا إلى الجزم بأن القديم في لغة العرب التي نزل بها القرآن هو المتقدم على غيره، فيقال هذا قديم للعتيق، وهذا حديث للجديد لا القدم الذي لا يسبقه العدم؛ ففي التنزيل قوله تعالى: ﴿ عَادَ كَالْعُرْجُونِ الْقَدِيمِ ﴾ [يس:39] قيل: وهو الذي يبقى إلى حين وجود العرجون الثاني، فإذا وجد الجديد قيل للأول قديم، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَإِذْلَمْ يَهْتَدُوا بِهِ فَسَيَقُولُونَ هَذَا إِفْكٌ قَدِيمٌ ﴾ [الأحقاف:11]، أي متقدم في الزمان. ثم لا ريب فيه أنه إذا كان مستعملا بمعنى المتقدم فمن تقدم على الحوادث كلها فهو أحق بالتقدم من غيره لكن أسماء الله تعالى هي الأسماء الحسنى التي تدل على خصوص يمدح به، والتقدم في اللغة مطلق لا يختص بالتقدم على الحوادث كلها، فلا يكون من الأسماء الحسنى، وجاء الشرع بإسمه 'الأول'، وهو أحسن من القديم؛ لأنه يشعر بأن ما بعده آيل إليه له بخلاف القديم، إلا أنه لما كان الله سبحانه وتعالى هو الفرد الأكمل في معنى القديم المتناول للأول فأطلقه المتكلمون عليه، فتأمل. ثم 'القيوم' يدل على معنى الأزلية والأبدية ما لا يدل عليه لفظ القديم، ويدل أيضاً على كونه موجودا بنفسه، وهو معنى كونه واجب الوجود، ولهذا المبنى المشتمل على حقائق المعنى
And the result is that His speech is eternal (qadīm) in essence and attributes, and eternality necessitates perpetuity; because whatever is established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible, as is derived from His statement 'He is the First (al-Awwal) and the Last (al-Ākhir)' (Surah al-Hadid, 57:3), meaning without beginning or end. As for 'al-Qadīm', it is not among the beautiful names, although the scholars of kalām apply it to Him—while many venerable Salaf denied it, as well as some of the eminent later scholars, among them Ibn Ḥazm, taking a firm stance that 'qadīm' in the language of the Arabs in which the Qur'an was revealed means that which precedes something else. So it is said 'this is qadīm' for the old, and 'this is ḥadīth' for the new, not the eternal (qidam) that is not preceded by non-existence. In the revelation, He says: 'like an old date-stalk' (Surah Yasin, 36:39) said: it is that which remains until the existence of the second date-stalk; so when the new exists, the first is called qadīm. And His statement: 'And when they are not guided by it, they will say: This is an ancient lie' (Surah al-Ahqaf, 46:11), meaning prior in time. Then there is no doubt that if it is used in the sense of 'prior', then He who is prior to all originated things is more deserving of priority than others. However, the names of Allah are the beautiful names (al-asmā' al-ḥusnā) that indicate a specific attribute for which He is praised. Priority (al-taqaddum) in language is absolute and does not specifically denote priority over all originated things, so it is not among the beautiful names. The Shari'a came with His name 'al-Awwal', which is better than 'al-Qadīm' because it indicates that what comes after is directed to Him, unlike 'al-Qadīm'. Nevertheless, since Allah—may He be glorified and exalted—is the perfect individual in the meaning of 'al-Qadīm' that encompasses 'al-Awwal', the mutakallimūn applied it to Him, so reflect. Then 'al-Qayyūm' indicates the meaning of pre-eternity and post-eternity which the word 'al-Qadīm' does not indicate, and also indicates His existence by Himself, which is the meaning of His being the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd). Hence this foundation that comprises the realities of meaning...
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage starts by concluding the previous discussion: God's speech is eternal and eternality implies necessary perpetuity. Then it moves to the main point: the name 'al-Qadīm' (the Eternal) is not among the beautiful names (al-asmā' al-ḥusnā) of Allah, although it has been used by mutakallimūn. Many of the Salaf, including Ibn Ḥazm, rejected it. The linguistic evidence: in Arabic, 'qadīm' means something that precedes something else, not something that is without beginning. The Qur'an uses it this way, e.g., 'old date-stalk' and 'ancient lie'. Priority (taqaddum) is a relative term; even if it applies to Allah in a superior way, it does not denote a specific praiseworthy attribute exclusive to Him. The Shari'a gives us the name 'al-Awwal', which is better because it implies that everything after Him depends on Him. However, because Allah is the perfect instance of 'qadīm' in the sense of being eternal a parte ante, the theologians have used it, but one should reflect on the difference. Finally, the name 'al-Qayyūm' is superior as it denotes both pre-eternity and post-eternity as well as self-subsistence, which is exactly the meaning of necessary existence. The passage ends abruptly but clearly shows the critical approach to divine names.

Key Terms
القديم
الأول
القيوم
الأسماء الحسنى
بقائية
أزلية
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'al-Qadīm' is a divine name from the Quran or Sunnah.
Correction: The passage shows that 'al-Qadīm' is a theological term, not a revealed name. The Quran uses 'al-Awwal' and the Sunnah has 'al-Qayyūm', which are more precise.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that 'al-Qadīm' is not among Allah's beautiful names?
A) Because it does not appear in the Qur'an
B) Because in Arabic it only means 'old' or 'prior' and does not specifically denote absolute priority over all originated things
C) Because the Salaf unanimously banned its use
D) Because it implies change in Allah

Key Takeaway
The term 'al-Qadīm' is a theological description, not a revealed name; the revealed names 'al-Awwal' and 'al-Qayyūm' more accurately convey Allah's eternal and self-sufficient nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah, do you use names that are firmly established in revelation, or have you adopted theological terms without checking? If you have used 'al-Qadīm' in supplication, will you now replace it with 'al-Awwal' and 'al-Qayyūm' as taught in this lesson?
?
The author emphasizes that 'al-Qayyūm' indicates Allah's self-subsistence and that all existence depends on Him. Do you truly rely on Allah as al-Qayyūm in your daily life, or do you unconsciously act as if you are self-sufficient? What is one area where you can consciously depend on Him starting today?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly praiseworthy term like 'al-Qadīm' can be inappropriate for describing Allah if it is not revealed. Are there any other attributes or descriptions you use for Allah that might be based on human speculation rather than the Quran and Sunnah? Will you commit to learning and using only the revealed names?

Why al-Qadim Is Not a Beautiful Name
Argument that al-Qadim is not among Allah's beautiful names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'al-Qadim' is not considered among the beautiful names of Allah, based on linguistic and scriptural analysis.

Core Idea
Al-Qadim means relative precedence, not absolute pre-eternity.

This lesson examines a nuanced theological argument about the divine name 'al-Qadim'. While theologians sometimes use it, many early scholars (salaf) rejected it as a beautiful name. The author explains the linguistic basis in Arabic and Quranic usage, and why 'al-Awwal' is more fitting. We'll trace the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the attribute of eternality
The passage opens by stating that Allah's attributes are eternal and that eternality implies perpetuity (impossibility of ceasing). This is supported by the verse 'He is the First and the Last'.
Note the connection between qidam (eternality) and baqa' (perpetuity).
2
Identify the main claim
The author then declares that 'al-Qadim' is not among the beautiful names, even though some theologians used it. Many salaf and later scholars like Ibn Hazm rejected it.
3
Examine the linguistic evidence
The author provides Quranic examples where 'qadim' is used to mean 'old' or 'preceding in time' (e.g., the old date-stalk, an ancient lie). This shows it does not denote absolute pre-eternity.
Contrast with the philosophical meaning of 'eternal'.
4
Distinguish between 'al-Qadim' and 'al-Awwal'
The name 'al-Awwal' (the First) is revealed and superior because it implies that everything after Him depends on Him, whereas 'al-Qadim' merely denotes precedence.
The author notes that theologians applied 'al-Qadim' because Allah embodies the concept in a perfect sense, but it remains not among the beautiful names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا هو بحسب المفهوم الذهني، ولا غيره بحسب الوجود الخارجي، فإن مفهوم الصفات غير مفهوم الذات إلا أنها لا تغايرها باعتبار ظهورها في الكائنات. والحاصل أن كلامه وهو قديم بذاته وصفاته والقديمية مستلزمة للبقائية؛ لأن ما ثبت قدمه يستحيل عدمه، كما هو مستفاد من قوله تعالى: ﴿ هُوَ الْاَوَّلُ وَالْآخِر [الحديد:٣]، أي بلا ابتداء ولا انتهاء. وأما القديم" فليس من الأسماء الحسنى وإن أطلقه عليه علماء الكلام مع أنه أنكره كثير السلف الكرام، وكذا بعض من الخلف الفخام، ومنهم ابن حزم ذهابا إلى الجزم بأن القديم في لغة العرب التي نزل بها القرآن هو المتقدم على غيره، فيقال هذا قديم للعتيق، وهذا حديث للجديد لا القدم الذي لا يسبقه العدم ؛ ففي التنزيل قوله تعالى : عَادَ كَالْعُرْجُوْنِ الْقَدِيمِ ﴾ [يس:٣٩] قيل: وهو الذي يبقى إلى حين وجود العرجون الثاني، فإذا وجد الجديد قيل للأول قديم، وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذْ لَمْ يَهْتَدُوا بِهِ فَسَيَقُولُونَ هذَا إفْكٌ قَدِيمٌ ﴾ [الاحقاف:١١]، أي متقدم في الزمان. ثم لا ريب فيه أنه إذا كان مستعملا بمعنى المتقدم فمن تقدم على الحوادث كلها فهو أحق بالتقدم من غيره لكن أسماء الله تعالى هي الأسماء الحسنى التي تدل على خصوص يمدح به، والتقدم في اللغة مطلق لا يختص بالتقدم على الحوادث كلها، فلا يكون من الأسماء الحسنى، وجاء الشرع بإسمه "الأول"، وهو أحسن من القديم؛ لأنه يشعر بأن ما بعده آيل إليه له بخلاف القديم، إلا أنه لما كان الله سبحانه وتعالى هو الفرد الأكمل في معنى القديم متابع ما المناول للأول فأطلقه المتكلمون عليه، فتأمل.
It is not according to the mental concept, nor other according to external existence. For the concept of attributes is different from the concept of the essence, but they do not differ from it by virtue of their manifestation in beings. The conclusion is that His speech is eternal in His essence and attributes, and eternality necessitates perpetuity; because whatever is established as eternal is impossible to cease, as is understood from His saying: 'He is the First and the Last' (al-Hadid 57:3), i.e., without beginning or end. As for 'al-Qadim', it is not among the beautiful names even though the scholars of kalam have applied it to Him, while many of the noble predecessors (salaf) denied it, as did some of the later distinguished scholars, among them Ibn Hazm, taking a definite stance that 'al-qadim' in the language of the Arabs in which the Quran was revealed is that which precedes others, so it is said 'this is qadim' for the old, and 'this is hadith' for the new, not the pre-eternity that is not preceded by non-existence. So in the Revelation: His saying: 'it becomes like an old dry date-stalk' (Ya-Seen 36:39) — it is said: it is that which remains until the existence of the second date-stalk, so when the new comes, the first is called qadim. And His saying: 'And when they are not guided by it, they will say: This is an ancient lie' (al-Ahqaf 46:11), i.e., preceding in time. Then there is no doubt that if it is used in the sense of precedence, then He who precedes all events is most deserving of precedence than others, but the names of Allah are the beautiful names that indicate a specific praiseworthy quality, and precedence in language is absolute, not specific to precedence over all events, so it is not among the beautiful names. And the revealed law came with His name 'al-Awwal', which is better than al-Qadim; because it implies that what comes after is dependent on Him, unlike al-Qadim. However, since Allah is the perfect individual in the meaning of qadim following what encompasses al-Awwal, the theologians applied it to Him, so reflect.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage analyzes the divine name 'al-Qadim' (the Ancient). The author begins by clarifying that the attribute of pre-eternity (qidam) is inherent to Allah's essence and attributes, and that eternality necessitates perpetuity, citing the verse 'He is the First and the Last.' Then he argues that the term 'al-Qadim' is not among the beautiful names (al-Asma' al-Husna) despite its use by kalam scholars. He explains that in the Arabic language of the Quran, 'qadim' means 'preceding in time' (as in the verse about the old date-stalk and the 'ancient lie'), not absolute pre-eternity that is not preceded by non-existence. Many salaf (including Ibn Hazm) rejected its application to Allah because it does not denote a specific praiseworthy meaning exclusive to divinity. The revealed name 'al-Awwal' (the First) is superior because it implies both priority and that all else derives from Him. The author notes that since Allah is the perfect embodiment of the concept of qadim in the sense of encompassing the meaning of 'al-Awwal', the theologians have applied it, but he cautions the reader to reflect on the nuance.

Key Terms
القديم (al-Qadim)
الأول (al-Awwal)
الأسماء الحسنى (beautiful names)
السلف (Salaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'al-Qadim' is one of Allah's beautiful names without qualification.
Correction: The author shows that 'al-Qadim' is not among the beautiful names because its linguistic meaning is relative precedence, not absolute pre-eternity, and the revealed name 'al-Awwal' is more appropriate.

Checkpoint
Why is 'al-Qadim' not considered among the beautiful names of Allah according to the author?
A) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because in Arabic it means precedence in time, not absolute pre-eternity.
C) Because the theologians rejected it.
D) Because it implies imperfection.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadim is not among Allah's beautiful names because its linguistic meaning is relative precedence; the revealed name al-Awwal more perfectly conveys Allah's absolute priority and other-dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Have you ever casually called Allah a name without verifying it from revelation? This lesson shows the precision required in naming Allah. Will you commit today to using only the names He has revealed or approved, and to teaching others to do the same?
?
The author ends with 'so reflect'. What specific reflection does this lesson demand from you right now? Are you willing to examine and correct any inadvertent irreverence in how you speak about Allah, even in your heart?

Al-Qayyum and the Greatest Name
Explanation of al-Qayyum and its role in the Greatest Name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the name al-Qayyum encompasses pre-eternity, self-existence, and the dependence of all creation, and why al-Hayy al-Qayyum is considered the Greatest Name.

Core Idea
Al-Hayy al-Qayyum together encompass all divine perfections.

This passage from the sharh explores the depth of the divine name al-Qayyum. The author explains that it indicates meanings not conveyed by al-Qadim, and argues that the combination of al-Hayy and al-Qayyum forms the Greatest Name of Allah. We will see how each name entails attributes of perfection and why they are central to all divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the contrast with al-Qadim.
2
Understand the meaning of al-Qayyum
Focus on how al-Qayyum indicates pre-eternity, everlastingness, and self-existence (necessary existence).
3
Examine the hadith support
The hadith about Ayat al-Kursi being the greatest verse supports that al-Hayy al-Qayyum is the Greatest Name.
Reflect on why this verse is so important.
4
Grasp the encompassing nature
See how al-Hayy necessitates all perfections and al-Qayyum includes self-sufficiency and sustaining others.
These two names cover all divine attributes.
5
Conclude about the Greatest Name
The author concludes that these two names are so comprehensive that they are likely the Greatest Name.
Allah knows best, but the evidence is strong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم "القيوم" يدل على معنى الأزلية والأبدية ما لا يدل عليه لفظ القديم، ويدل أيضاً على كونه موجودا بنفسه، وهو معنى كونه واجب الوجود، ولهذا المبنى المشتمل على حقائق المعنى. قيل: الحي القيوم هو الاسم الأعظم، ويؤيده ما صح عنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم: ((أن قوله تعالى اللهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ الْحَيُّ الْقَيُّومُ ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٥٥] أعظم آية في القرآن)). ويقويه أن هذين الاسمين مدار الأسماء الحسنى كلها وإليهما يرجع جميع معانيها، فإن الحياة مستلزمة لجميع صفات الكمال، فلا يختلف عنها صفة منها إلا لضعف الحياة، فإذا كانت حياته أكمل حياة وأتمها استلزم إثباتها إثبات كل كمال يضاهيه كمال الحياة. وأما "القيوم" فهو متضمن كمال غناه وكمال قدرته وافتقار غيره إليه في ذاته وصفاته إيجاداً وإمداداً، فإنه القائم بنفسه فلا يحتاج إلى غيره بوجه من الوجوه المقيم لغيره فلا قيام لغيره إلا بإقامته فانتظم هذان الاسمان صفات الكمال على الوجه الأتم، فلا يبعد أن يكونا الاسم الأعظم، والله سبحانه أعلم.
Then 'al-Qayyum' indicates the meaning of pre-eternity and everlastingness that the word 'al-Qadim' does not indicate, and it also indicates His being existent by Himself, which is the meaning of His being necessary of existence. And for this structure encompassing the realities of the meaning, it is said: 'al-Hayy al-Qayyum' is the Greatest Name, and it is supported by what is authentically reported from him (peace be upon him): 'That His saying: Allah, there is no god but He, al-Hayy al-Qayyum (al-Baqara 2:255) is the greatest verse in the Quran.' And it is strengthened by the fact that these two names are the pivot of all the beautiful names and all their meanings return to them. For life (al-Hayat) necessitates all attributes of perfection; no attribute is absent from them except due to the weakness of life. So if His life is the most perfect and complete life, its affirmation entails the affirmation of every perfection that is commensurate with the perfection of life. As for 'al-Qayyum', it includes the perfection of His self-sufficiency and the perfection of His power and the need of everything else to Him in their essence and attributes, both in origination and sustenance. For He is self-subsistent, not needing anything in any respect, while He sustains others; nothing has subsistence except through His sustaining. Thus these two names encompass the attributes of perfection in the most complete manner, so it is not unlikely that they are the Greatest Name, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage discusses the name 'al-Qayyum' and its relationship to the Greatest Name. The author begins by contrasting 'al-Qayyum' with 'al-Qadim': whereas 'al-Qadim' denotes precedence, 'al-Qayyum' denotes pre-eternity (azaliyya) and everlastingness (abadiyya), as well as self-existence (wujud bi-nafsihi) which is the meaning of necessary existence (wajib al-wujud). He then cites the report that the name 'al-Hayy al-Qayyum' is the Greatest Name, supported by the hadith declaring Ayat al-Kursi the greatest Quranic verse. He explains why these two names are central: life (al-Hayy) necessitates all attributes of perfection because any missing attribute would imply weakness in life, and since Allah's life is perfect, all perfections are affirmed. Meanwhile, 'al-Qayyum' contains self-sufficiency, power, and the dependence of all creation on Him for existence and sustenance. The author concludes that these two names together exhaustively encompass all attributes of perfection, so they likely constitute the Greatest Name of Allah.

Key Terms
القيوم (al-Qayyum)
الحي (al-Hayy)
الاسم الأعظم (the Greatest Name)
آية الكرسي (Ayat al-Kursi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Greatest Name is a specific secret word that one must discover through esoteric knowledge.
Correction: The author shows that the Greatest Name is not a hidden word but the combination of al-Hayy and al-Qayyum, which together encompass all divine attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does 'al-Qayyum' indicate more than 'al-Qadim'?
A) It indicates both pre-eternity and everlastingness.
B) It indicates independence and sustaining others.
C) It includes the meaning of necessary existence.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
The name al-Qayyum indicates self-existence and sustenance, and together with al-Hayy it encompasses all divine attributes, making it the Greatest Name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Ayat al-Kursi, do you actually feel that you are affirming that Allah is al-Hayy al-Qayyum—that He is your sole sustainer every moment? Or have you become so familiar with the words that they no longer shake your soul? What would it take for you to truly live with the awareness that without Him you would cease to exist?
?
Is there any area of your life where you act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that your very existence depends on the Qayyum? Write down one specific dependency you have ignored and commit to acknowledging it in your daily prayers.
?
This lesson argues that al-Hayy and al-Qayyum together encompass all divine perfections. If this is your standard of worship, then what imperfect attribute do you still ascribe to Allah in your heart? Are you ready to repent from any deficiency you have imagined in Him?

Al-Hayy Al-Qayyum: The Greatest Name
Commentary presenting evidence that Al-Hayy Al-Qayyum is the Greatest Name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the name Al-Hayy Al-Qayyum is considered the Greatest Name based on scriptural and rational evidence.

Core Idea
Life and self-subsistence are the foundation of all divine perfections.

This passage presents the view that the name Al-Hayy Al-Qayyum is the Greatest Name. It combines prophetic testimony with a rational analysis of divine attributes. Let's explore both arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice that the author begins with 'قيل' indicating this is a reported view.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
The passage provides two main evidences: a hadith about Ayat al-Kursi and a rational argument about life and qayyum encompassing all attributes. Find them in the text.
The hadith is the first evidence; the rational argument starts with 'ويقويه'.
3
Reflect on how the two names encompass perfection
Consider why life entails all perfections and how 'Al-Qayyum' implies self-sufficiency and power. How do these two together cover all attributes?
This reflection helps you see the depth of the rational argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل: الحي القيوم هو الاسم الأعظم، ويؤيده ما صح عنه صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم: ((أن قوله تعالى اللهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ الْحَيُّ الْقَيُّومُ ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٥٥] أعظم آية في القرآن)). ويقويه أن هذين الاسمين مدار الأسماء الحسنى كلها وإليهما يرجع جميع معانيها، فإن الحياة مستلزمة لجميع صفات الكمال، فلا يختلف عنها صفة منها إلا لضعف الحياة، فإذا كانت حياته أكمل حياة وأتمها استلزم إثباتها إثبات كل كمال يضاهيه كمال الحياة. وأما "القيوم" فهو متضمن كمال غناه وكمال قدرته وافتقار غيره إليه في ذاته وصفاته إيجاداً وإمداداً، فإنه القائم بنفسه فلا يحتاج إلى غيره بوجه من الوجوه المقيم لغيره فلا قيام لغيره إلا بإقامته فانتظم هذان الاسمان صفات الكمال على الوجه الأتم، فلا يبعد أن يكونا الاسم الأعظم، والله سبحانه أعلم.
It is said: Al-Hayy Al-Qayyum is the Greatest Name, and it is supported by what is authentically reported from him (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him): that the saying of Allah Most High 'Allahu la ilaha illa Huwa Al-Hayy Al-Qayyum' [2:255] is the greatest verse in the Quran. And it is strengthened by the fact that these two names are the pivot of all the beautiful names and all their meanings return to them. For life entails all attributes of perfection; no attribute is absent from it except due to weakness of life. So when His life is the most perfect and complete life, its affirmation necessitates the affirmation of every perfection that matches the perfection of life. As for 'Al-Qayyum', it comprises the perfection of His self-sufficiency and the perfection of His power, and the dependence of others upon Him in their essence and attributes, in creation and sustenance. For He is self-subsisting, not needing anyone in any way, and the establisher of others – none has subsistence except through His establishment. Thus these two names encompass the attributes of perfection in the most complete manner, so it is not far that they are the Greatest Name. And Allah the Exalted knows best.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is an argument that the name 'Al-Hayy Al-Qayyum' is the greatest name of Allah. The author begins by stating that it is said (قيل) that this is the Greatest Name, indicating it is a reported view. He then provides two pieces of evidence. First, the hadith from the Prophet that Ayat al-Kursi is the greatest verse, and that verse includes these two names. Second, a rational argument that life is the basis of all perfections, so the most perfect life entails all perfections. And 'Al-Qayyum' implies self-sufficiency and power over others. Thus the two names together encompass all perfections, making them the Greatest Name. The author concludes with 'Allah knows best', showing humility.

Key Terms
الاسم الأعظم
الحياة
القيوم
صفات الكمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The name Al-Hayy Al-Qayyum is a strong candidate for the Greatest Name because it synthesizes scriptural testimony and logical encompassment of all perfections.

Quranic Stories as Eternal Divine Speech
Explanation that Quranic stories are Allah's eternal speech informing about them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quranic accounts of prophets and their enemies are not reports of past events but are Allah's eternal speech, pre-recorded in the Preserved Tablet before creation.

Core Idea
Quranic stories are eternal information, not created reports.

This passage from the commentary on Fiqh al-Akbar addresses a potential misunderstanding: do the Quranic stories about Moses, Pharaoh, and others mean that Allah's speech is created and dependent on time? The author explains that these narratives are part of Allah's eternal speech, pre-existing in the Preserved Tablet, and that He is simply informing us about them. This reinforces the doctrine of the uncreatedness of the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style of the commentary.
2
Identify the two categories mentioned
The passage mentions what Allah says about prophets (specifically Moses) and about enemies (Pharaoh and Iblis). Note the special significance given to Moses and the order of mention.
3
Understand the core doctrine
The key claim is that all these utterances are Allah's eternal speech, existing in the Preserved Tablet before creation. They are not created after the events happened.
This is a central point in Sunni theology regarding the Quran.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider why someone might think the Quranic stories are created. The passage clarifies that divine knowledge and speech are eternal, not dependent on temporal events.
This ties to the broader attribute of speech (kalam).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكره الله تعالى فى (القرآن أي المنزل والفرقان المكمل عن موسى وغيره من الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام) أي إخبارا منهم أو حكاية عنهم وعن فرعون وإبليس) أي ونحوهما من الأعداء الأغبياء، وفى تخصيص موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام إيماء إلى أنه صاحب التكليم والكلام، وفي تقديم فرعون إشعار بأنه في مقام التلبيس أقوى من إبليس. فإنّ ذلك (أى ما ذكر من النوعين) كلّه (على ما في نسخة أى جمعيه) كلام الله تعالى أي القديم (إخباراً عنهم) أي وفق ما قد كتب من الكلمات الدالة عليه في اللوح المحفوظ قبل خلق السماء والأرض والروح، لا بكلام حادث حصل بعد علم حادث عند سمعه من موسى وعيسى وغيرهما من الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام ومن فرعون وإبليس وهامان وقارون وسائر
And what Allah the Exalted mentioned in the Quran (i.e., the revealed Word and the Criterion completed) about Moses and other prophets, peace and blessings be upon them, and about Pharaoh and Iblis – meaning informing from them or narrating about them and about Pharaoh and Iblis, i.e., and the likes of them from the foolish enemies. And in specifying Moses, peace and blessings be upon him, there is an indication that he is the one who was spoken to and has speech, and in giving precedence to Pharaoh there is a suggestion that he is stronger in the position of deception than Iblis. Then that (i.e., what was mentioned of both types) entirely (according to a copy, i.e., both of them) is the speech of Allah, i.e., the eternal (informing about them), i.e., according to what has been recorded of the indicative words in the Preserved Tablet before the creation of the heavens, the earth, and the spirit, not by a created speech that occurred after a created knowledge when he heard from Moses, Jesus, and other prophets, peace be upon them, and from Pharaoh, Iblis, Haman, Qarun, and others.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the original text of Fiqh al-Akbar that says 'وما ذكره الله تعالى في القرآن عن موسى وغيره من الأنبياء وعن فرعون وإبليس'. The commentator explains that this refers to what Allah informs about them or narrates from them. He then notes the significance of specifying Moses, indicating his high status as one spoken to directly, and presenting Pharaoh before Iblis because Pharaoh is more deceptive. Then he emphasizes that all such Quranic utterances are Allah's eternal speech, in accordance with what was written in the Preserved Tablet before creation. They are not created speech that came into being after Allah heard them from the prophets or enemies. This reinforces the doctrine of the uncreatedness of the Quran.

Key Terms
كلام الله القديم
اللوح المحفوظ
إخبار
الكلام الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic stories about past prophets and enemies are created reports that Allah made after the events occurred.
Correction: The passage clarifies that these stories are part of Allah's eternal speech; they were recorded in the Preserved Tablet before creation and are not temporally generated.

Checkpoint
When did Allah's speech about Pharaoh and Iblis occur according to this passage?
A) After the events happened
B) At the time of revelation
C) From eternity, before creation
D) When the Prophet recited it

Key Takeaway
Quranic narratives are not created accounts; they are Allah's eternal speech pre-existing in the Preserved Tablet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the story of Musa in the Quran, do you realize you are hearing Allah's eternal speech, not a historical report? How would that change the way you listen and respond in prayer?
?
The Quran pre-eternally records the words of Pharaoh and Iblis. What does that tell you about Allah’s complete knowledge of your own struggles with your inner nafs and external temptations? Will that make you more vigilant or more trusting in His aid?
?
If the Quranic stories are not created after the events but are from the Preserved Tablet before time, what does that imply about the reality and weight of every word you hear? Will you let that truth transform your daily relationship with the Quran?

All Divine Speech is Uncreated and Equal in Status
Commentary explaining that all divine speech, including narratives and attributes, is equally eternal and uncreated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every part of the Quran and all forms of divine speech are equally eternal and uncreated, with no distinction between narrative verses and verses about God's attributes.

Core Idea
All divine speech is one eternal attribute – no part is created.

This commentary from the Manah al-Rawd al-Azhar clarifies a critical point in Islamic theology: God's speech is not a mixture of created and uncreated. The author explains that whether Allah informs us about past prophets or describes His own attributes, it all originates from the same eternal speech recorded in the Preserved Tablet. Let's explore how this principle answers common misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the commentary uses a very dense style with parenthetical clarifications.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where the commentator states that there is no difference between different types of divine speech. This is the central thesis of the passage.
3
Understand the supporting analogy
Notice the reference to the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz) and the negation of temporal hearing. This shows that divine speech is pre-eternal and not dependent on events.
4
Consider the misconception
The common misunderstanding is that historical narratives might be created after the event. The passage directly refutes this by equating all types of verses.
This misconception is addressed in the checkpoint question.
5
Reflect on implications
Think about why this doctrine matters: if any part of the Quran were created, it would imply temporality in God and undermine the divine origin of revelation.
This connects to the broader creedal context of the Fiqh al-Akbar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنّ ذلك كله كلام الله تعالى إخباراً عنهم، (فإن ذلك) أى ما ذكر من النوعين (كلّه) على ما في نسخة أى جمعيه كلام الله تعالى أي القديم (إخباراً عنهم) أي وفق ما قد كتب من الكلمات الدالة عليه في اللوح المحفوظ قبل خلق السماء والأرض والروح، لا بكلام حادث حصل بعد علم حادث عند سمعه من موسى وعيسى وغيرهما من الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام ومن فرعون وإبليس وهامان وقارون وسائر الأعداء، فإذا لا فرق بين إخبار الله تعالى عن أخبارهم وأحوالهم وأسرارهم كسورة تبت وآية القتال ونحوها، وبين إظهار الله تعالى من صفات ذاته وأفعاله وخلق مصنوعاته كآية الكرسي وسورة الإخلاص وأمثالها، وبين الآيات الآفاقية والأنفسية في كون كل منها كلامه وصفته الأقدسية الأنفسية.
So all of that is the speech of Allah the Exalted, informing about them. (So all of that) means what was mentioned of the two types, all of it – according to one copy – i.e., both of them are the speech of Allah the Exalted, i.e., the Eternal. (Informing about them) i.e., according to what has been written of the words indicating it in the Preserved Tablet before the creation of the heavens, the earth, and the spirit; not by created speech that occurred after a created knowledge when He heard from Moses, Jesus, and other prophets, peace be upon them, and from Pharaoh, Iblis, Haman, Qarun, and other enemies. Then there is no difference between Allah's informing about their news, states, and secrets, like Surah Tabbat and the verse of fighting and the like, and between Allah's manifesting from the attributes of His Self and His actions and creating His creatures, like Ayat al-Kursi and Surah al-Ikhlas and their likes, and between the cosmic signs and the personal signs, in that all of them are His speech and His most holy personal attribute.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the statement 'So all of that is the speech of Allah the Exalted, informing about them.' The commentator first glosses each term: 'all of that' refers to the two types mentioned earlier, and 'informing about them' means according to what was preordained in the Preserved Tablet, not as a result of a temporal hearing. He then extends the point: there is no distinction between different categories of divine speech – whether it reports events of prophets and enemies, or describes God's attributes and acts, or provides signs in the cosmos and within ourselves. All of it is equally God's eternal speech. The commentary emphasizes that the Quran, in all its verses, is uncreated and timeless.

Key Terms
كلام الله
قديم
اللوح المحفوظ
الآيات الآفاقية
الآيات الأنفسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only verses about God's essence are eternal, while historical narratives are created in time.
Correction: The commentary explicitly states there is no distinction; all are equally God's eternal speech recorded in the Preserved Tablet.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the status of the verses that narrate stories of past nations?
A) They are created to reflect events.
B) They are eternal like verses about attributes.
C) They are a mixture of created and uncreated.
D) They are only metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
All verses of the Quran, whether narrating history or describing God, are equally the uncreated, eternal speech of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the story of Pharaoh or Iblis in the Quran, do you ever unconsciously feel that those verses are less divine than verses about Allah's mercy? How does this lesson challenge that feeling, and what will you do to change your perception starting today?
?
The passage says there is no difference between cosmic and personal signs as Allah's speech. In your daily life, which type do you tend to pay less attention to? What practical step can you take tonight to treat both with equal reverence?
?
If every single letter of the Quran is equally the eternal speech of Allah, how can you justify skimming over certain chapters or verses in your recitation? What will you change in your next recitation to honor that truth?

The Uncreated Nature of God's Speech
Commentary proving God's speech uncreated and Quran eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran is considered eternal as God's speech and how it differs from the created speech of prophets.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal, the Quran is His speech; prophets' speech is created.

This passage from the commentary on Fiqh al-Akbar establishes the fundamental distinction between God's uncreated speech and the created speech of His prophets. The author explains that the Quran is not metaphorical speech but real, eternal speech, supported by rational and scriptural arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall flow.
2
Identify the three claims
Find the three main claims: God's speech uncreated, prophets' speech created, and the Quran eternal.
The passage states these at the beginning.
3
Understand the argument from attribute
Notice the argument that the attribute (speech) follows the described (God), so since God is eternal, His speech is eternal.
Also note the analogy with hadith.
4
Reflect on scriptural proofs
Observe the Quranic verses cited as evidence that the Quran is God's speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلام الله تعالى غير مخلوق، وكلام موسى وغيره من المخلوقين مخلوق، والقرآن كلام الله تعالى فهو قديم لا كلامهم. ومجمل الكلام قوله على ما في نسخة وكلام الله تعالى) أي ما ينسب إليه سبحانه (غير مخلوق أي ولا حادث وكلام) (موسى) أي ولو كان مع ربه (وغيره) أي وكذا كلام غيره من المخلوقين) أي كسائر الأنبياء والمرسلين والملائكة المقربين (مخلوق) أي حادث بعد كونهم مخلوقين. والقرآن) كلام الله تعالى أي بالحقيقة كما قال الطحاوي رحمه الله لا بالمجاز كما قال غيره؛ لأن ما كان مجازا يصح نفيه وهنا لا يصح. وأجيب بأن الشرع إذا ورد بإطلاقه فيما يجب اعتقاده لا يصح نفيه فهو (قديم كذاته لا) كلامهم فإنه حادث مثلهم، إذ النعت تابع لمنعوته وإنما يقال : المنظوم العبراني الذي هو التوراة والمنظوم العربي الذي هو القرآن كلامه سبحانه؛ لأن كلماتهما وآياتهما أدلة كلامه وعلامات مرامه، ولأن مبدأ نظمها الله تعالى ألا ترى أنك إذا قرأت حديثا من الأحاديث قلت هذا الذي قرأته وذكرته ليس قولي من بل قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم؛ لأن مبدأ نظم ذلك القول من الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام. ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَفَتَطْمَعُونَ أَن يُؤْمِنُوا لَكُمْ وَقَدْ كَانَ فَرِيقٌ مِنْهُمْ يَسْمَعُونَ كَلِمَ اللهِ ﴾ [البقرة : ٧٥] ، وقوله عزوجل: ﴿وَإِنْ أَحَدٌ مِنَ الْمُشْرِكِينَ اسْتَجَارَكَ فَاجِرَهُ حَتَّى يَسْمَعَ كَلِمَ اللهِ ثُمَّ ابْلِغْهُ مَأْمَنَهُ ﴾ [التوبة : ٦] . واعلم أن ما جاء في كلام الإمام الأعظم وغيره من علماء الأنام من تكفير القائل بخلق القرآن فمحمول على كفران النعمة لا كفر الخروج من الملة، بخلاف المعتزلة في هذه المسألة،
And the speech of Allah the Exalted is uncreated, and the speech of Moses and other created beings is created, and the Qur'an is the speech of Allah the Exalted, so it is eternal, not their speech. And the summary of the speech: His saying according to a copy: 'And the speech of Allah the Exalted' i.e., what is attributed to Him, Exalted is He, 'uncreated' i.e., not originated; 'and the speech of Moses' i.e., even when he was with his Lord, 'and of others' i.e., likewise the speech of other created beings, i.e., like the rest of the prophets, messengers, and close angels, 'is created' i.e., originated after they were created. 'And the Qur'an' is the speech of Allah the Exalted, i.e., in reality as al-Tahawi said, not metaphorically as others said; because what is metaphorical can be denied, but here it cannot be denied. And it was answered that when the Law comes with an absolute term concerning what must be believed, its denial is not permissible, so it is 'eternal like His essence, not' their speech, for it is originated like them, since the attribute follows the one described. And it is said: the Hebrew composition which is the Torah and the Arabic composition which is the Qur'an are His speech, Exalted is He, because their words and verses are evidences of His speech and signs of His intention, and because the origin of their composition is Allah the Exalted. Do you not see that if you read a hadith, you say, 'What I have read and mentioned is not my words, but the words of the Messenger of Allah' because the origin of the composition of that speech is from the Messenger. And from that is His saying: 'Do you hope that they will believe you, while a party of them used to hear the word of Allah?' [2:75], and His saying: 'And if any of the polytheists seeks your protection, then grant him protection so that he may hear the word of Allah' [9:6]. And know that what has come in the speech of al-Imam al-A'zam and other scholars of the ummah regarding takfir of the one who says the Qur'an is created is carried upon ingratitude for the blessing, not apostasy from the religion, contrary to the Mu'tazila concerning this issue.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the creedal statement that God's speech is uncreated and the Quran is eternal. The commentator begins by stating the ruling: God's speech is uncreated, while the speech of created beings like Moses is created; the Quran is God's speech and therefore eternal, not created like human speech. He then glosses each part of the statement from the matn. He emphasizes that the Quran is God's speech in reality, not metaphorically, as stated by Imam al-Tahawi, because metaphorical statements can be denied, but here denial is not possible. He provides an argument: when the Shari'a uses an absolute term regarding matters of belief, it cannot be denied. He further explains that the attribute follows the described, so just as God is eternal, His speech is eternal. He then draws an analogy: the Torah and Quran are called God's speech because their words and verses are signs of His speech, and the origin of their composition is from God, just as when we relate a hadith, we attribute it to the Prophet, not to ourselves. He supports this with Quranic verses that mention 'hearing the word of Allah.' Finally, he notes that Imam Abu Hanifa and other scholars who declared the one who says the Quran is created as a disbeliever meant it as kufr of ingratitude (kufran al-ni'mah), not as apostasy from the religion, contrary to the view of the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
كلام الله
قديم
مخلوق
القرآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is created because it is composed of Arabic words and revealed in time.
Correction: The Quran is eternal as the uncreated speech of Allah; its composition originates from Allah, not from human invention.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the Quran considered eternal?
A) Because it is composed of Arabic words.
B) Because it is the speech of God, who is eternal.
C) Because it was written in the Preserved Tablet.
D) Because the Prophet said so.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is the eternal, uncreated speech of Allah, not metaphorical or created like the speech of creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly feel that you are uttering the speech of the Eternal One? How would your recitation change if you were fully conscious that these words are not created, but are from the One who never began?
?
You defend the Quran as the word of Allah, but have you ever pondered the profound difference between created words and uncreated words? Does this truth affect your trust in every verse, knowing it is not subject to change or error?
?
If someone were to argue that the Quran is merely a human composition, would you have the conviction to refute them using the reasoning from this lesson? Examine your heart: are you willing to stand firm on this foundational belief even if it costs you social acceptance?

The Nuance of Takfir on the Quran's Creation
Clarification on the meaning of takfir regarding the creation of the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction Imam Abu Hanifa made between takfir of ingratitude and apostasy regarding the belief in the creation of the Quran.

Core Idea
Declaring the Quran created is kufr of ingratitude, not apostasy, except for the Mu'tazila.

The final part of this commentary addresses a sensitive issue: declaring someone a disbeliever for saying the Quran is created. The author clarifies that Imam Abu Hanifa's takfir was not apostasy but ingratitude, except for the Mu'tazila who held this belief as a foundational doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the key terms 'kufran al-ni'mah' and 'kufr al-khuruj min al-milla'.
2
Understand the two types of kufr
The passage distinguishes between kufr of ingratitude and kufr of apostasy. Know the difference.
The first is not leaving the religion, the second is.
3
Note the exception of Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila are excluded because they made this belief a core tenet, so their takfir is apostasy.

Allah's Speech is Uncreated
Sanusi distinguishes divine speech from created speech and declares the Quran eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental Sunni belief that Allah's speech is uncreated and distinct from the created speech of all creatures.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is eternal; all other speech is created.

This lesson begins with a clear distinction between divine and created speech. The author states plainly that Allah's speech is not created, while the speech of prophets and angels is created. This sets the foundation for understanding the eternal nature of the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the three key statements: Allah's speech uncreated, creatures' speech created, and the Quran eternal.
The passage uses a tafsir-style expansion of the initial statement.
2
Identify the two categories of speech
Find where the passage distinguishes between divine speech (attributed to Allah) and created speech (of Moses and others). Note that even Moses' speech during his conversation with Allah is created.
The category of divine speech is uncreated and eternal.
3
Connect to the Quran
Observe that the passage concludes the Quran is eternal because it is Allah's speech. This will be expanded in the next lesson.
The Quran is not like the speech of creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلام الله تعالى غير مخلوق، وكلام موسى وغيره من المخلوقين مخلوق، والقرآن كلام الله تعالى فهو قديم لا كلامهم. ومجمل الكلام قوله على ما في نسخة وكلام الله تعالى أي ما ينسب إليه سبحانه غير مخلوق أي ولا حادث وكلام موسى أي ولو كان مع ربه وغيره أي وكذا كلام غيره من المخلوقين أي كسائر الأنبياء والمرسلين والملائكة المقربين مخلوق أي حادث بعد كونهم مخلوقين. والقرآن كلام الله تعالى أي بالحقيقة كما قال الطحاوي رحمه الله لا بالمجاز كما قال غيره؛ لأن ما كان مجازا يصح نفيه وهنا لا يصح. وأجيب بأن الشرع إذا ورد بإطلاقه فيما يجب اعتقاده لا يصح نفيه فهو قديم كذاته لا كلامهم.
And the speech of Allah Most High is uncreated, and the speech of Moses and others of the creatures is created, and the Quran is the speech of Allah Most High, so it is eternal, not their speech. And the summary of the speech is his statement according to one copy: 'And the speech of Allah Most High' i.e., what is attributed to Him, Glorified, is uncreated, i.e., not originated, 'and the speech of Moses' i.e., even if it was with his Lord, 'and others' i.e., likewise the speech of other creatures i.e., like the rest of the prophets and messengers and the close angels, is created i.e., originated after they were created. And the Quran is the speech of Allah Most High, i.e., in reality as al-Tahawi (may Allah have mercy on him) said, not metaphorically as others said; because what is metaphorical can be negated, but here it cannot. And it was answered that when the Law (shar‘) uses it absolutely in what must be believed, it cannot be negated. So it is eternal like His essence, not their speech.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines the core Sunni belief that Allah's speech (kalam) is uncreated (ghayr makhluq) and that the Quran, being His speech, is eternal (qadim). The author begins by stating that the speech of Allah is not created, while the speech of creatures like Moses is created. He then elaborates using an expanded paraphrase: the speech attributed to Allah is uncreated and not originated, whereas the speech of creatures is originated after their creation. Regarding the Quran, he affirms it is Allah's speech in reality, not metaphorically, citing Imam al-Tahawi. The author anticipates an objection: if it were metaphorical, it could be negated, but since the Law (shar‘) uses the term absolutely in matters of belief, negation is not permissible. Therefore, the Quran is eternal like Allah's essence, not like the speech of creatures.

غير مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because the Quran was revealed in time, it is created.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the Quran as Allah's speech is eternal, not created, because it is attributed to Allah, whose attributes are uncreated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the speech of Moses when he spoke with his Lord?
A) It is uncreated because it was with Allah.
B) It is created because Moses is a creature.
C) It is eternal like the Quran.
D) It is neither created nor uncreated.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is uncreated and eternal; the speech of all creatures, including prophets, is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that Allah's speech is eternal and uncreated, unlike all human speech. When you recite the Quran, do you treat it as the very words of Allah, eternal and uncreated, or do you unconsciously lower it to the level of human words? Will you commit today to reciting with the awe that these words are from the timeless attribute of your Lord?
?
If even the speech of a prophet like Moses, who spoke directly to Allah, is considered created, what does that say about the absolute transcendence of Allah's attribute of speech? How does this realization humble you when you speak about Allah or in His presence? Will you guard your tongue from trivial talk about divine matters?
?
You believe the Quran is eternal. But does that belief translate into action? When you hear an ayah, do you pause as if the Lord of the worlds is addressing you at that moment? Or is it just another recitation? What one change will you make in your daily engagement with the Quran because it is the eternal speech of Allah?

The Quran is Eternal in Reality, Not Metaphor
Sanusi argues that the Quran is Allah's speech in reality, not metaphor, using logical and legal reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the Quran is Allah's speech in reality, not metaphorically, and why metaphorical interpretation is rejected based on the Law's absolute usage.

Core Idea
The Quran is literally Allah's eternal speech, not a metaphor.

After establishing that Allah's speech is uncreated, the author turns to the nature of the Quran. He affirms, following Imam al-Tahawi, that the Quran is Allah's speech in reality, not metaphorically. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this and the response to a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on reality vs. metaphor
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the explicit claim: the Quran is Allah's speech in reality (haqiqa), not metaphor (majaz).
The author cites Imam al-Tahawi to support this.
2
Understand the logical argument
Identify the reason given: metaphorical statements can be negated, but the Quran cannot be negated as Allah's speech.
This is a key rational proof against metaphorical interpretation.
3
See the answer to the objection
Note how the author preempts an objection: if it were metaphor, it could be negated; but the Law uses the term absolutely in matters of belief, so negation is invalid.
Thus the Quran is eternal like Allah's essence.

No Distinction Between Quranic Verses
The author argues there is no difference between narrative and theological verses in being Allah's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every verse of the Quran, regardless of its subject matter, is equally the uncreated speech of Allah and His essential attribute.

Core Idea
Every verse of the Quran is equally and fully Allah's speech.

In some theological views, there might be a temptation to differentiate between verses that talk about created things and verses that speak about Allah's essence. The author of Fiqh al-Akbar decisively removes this distinction. This brief but powerful passage asserts the uniform divine origin of every single Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note its structure
Read the Arabic passage once to see the three categories the author lists. Then read the translation to grasp the central claim.
Notice the phrase 'لا فرق' (no difference) is the thesis.
2
Identify the three types of verses
The passage compares: (1) verses about the reports of past nations, (2) verses about divine attributes and creation, (3) cosmic and self-referential signs. Understand that these are not separate kinds of speech.
Examples include Surah Tabbat, Ayat al-Kursi, and Surah al-Ikhlas.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
If all these are equally Allah's speech and His essential attribute, then no part of the Quran can be considered created. This upholds the orthodox view that the Quran is uncreated.
This counters the Mu'tazilite claim that only some Quranic verses are eternal.
4
Connect to your broader understanding
Consider how this principle affects your view of the Quran: every verse, whether about history, law, or theology, carries the same divine authority and eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا لا فرق بين إخبار الله تعالى عن أخبارهم وأحوالهم وأسرارهم كسورة تبت وآية القتال ونحوها، وبين إظهار الله تعالى من صفات ذاته وأفعاله وخلق مصنوعاته كآية الكرسي وسورة الإخلاص وأمثالها، وبين الآيات الآفاقية والأنفسية في كون كل منها كلامه وصفته الأقدسية الأنفسية.
Thus there is no difference between the information of Allah Most High about their reports, states, and secrets like Surah Tabbat and the verse of fighting and similar, and between the manifestation of Allah Most High from the attributes of His essence, His acts, and the creation of His creatures like the Ayat al-Kursi and Surah al-Ikhlas and their likes, and between the cosmic and self-referential verses in that each of them is His speech and His most holy essential attribute.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage closes an objection or potential doubt: some might think that verses dealing with created beings (like stories of enemies) are somehow less divine or inherently created. The author refutes this by asserting that there is no distinction—all Quranic verses, whether they recount the affairs of creatures (e.g., Surah al-Masad 'Tabbat', battle verses) or declare divine attributes and actions (e.g., Ayat al-Kursi, Surah al-Ikhlas), or are cosmic signs or signs within the self, are equally His speech and His essential attribute. The key point is that the Quran in its entirety is the uncreated speech of Allah, regardless of subject matter.

No Real Dispute with Mu'tazila on Createdness of Speech
The author explains that the dispute with the Mu'tazila is only apparent due to different definitions of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent conflict between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila over the createdness of the Quran is resolved by distinguishing between verbal and internal speech.

Core Idea
The dispute is terminological: both sides agree on created verbal speech.

The debate over whether the Quran is created or eternal is often cited as a major point of division between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila. However, this passage from Sanusi's commentary suggests that the disagreement is not as deep as it appears. By carefully distinguishing between two types of speech—verbal and internal—the author shows that both camps actually agree on the key points, and the real issue is whether the Mu'tazila accept the concept of an eternal internal speech attribute of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two key terms: 'kalam lafzi' (verbal speech) and 'kalam nafsi' (internal speech).
The passage is short but dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two claims
Notice that the author first states there is no dispute 'in this matter' (fi hadhihi al-qadiyya). Then he gives two reasons: Ahl al-Sunna agree on the createdness of verbal speech, and the Mu'tazila would agree on the eternity of internal speech if they accepted its existence.
The second claim is conditional: 'if it were established for them by decisive proof.'
3
Understand the resolution
Reflect on how this resolves the apparent conflict. Both groups actually affirm the same thing about created verbal speech. The only remaining disagreement is about whether Allah has an attribute of internal speech that is eternal.
The Mu'tazila reject internal speech as an attribute, so the condition in the passage is not fulfilled.
4
Apply to the Quran
Consider how this applies to the Quran itself. The physical words and sounds (the mushaf or recited Quran) are created, but the eternal attribute of speech that they express is uncreated. This clarifies the Sunni position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل التحقيق أن لا نزاع في هذه القضية؛ إذ لا خلاف لأهل السنة في حدوث الكلام اللفظى، ولا نزاع للمعتزلة في قدم الكلام النفسي لو ثبت عندهم بالدليل القطعي.
Rather, the truth is that there is no dispute in this matter; for there is no disagreement from Ahl al-Sunna regarding the createdness of verbal speech (kalam lafzi), and there is no dispute from the Mu‘tazila regarding the eternity of internal speech (kalam nafsi) if it were established for them by decisive proof.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage makes an important conciliatory point: the famous conflict between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila over the createdness of the Quran is largely terminological. Ahl al-Sunna themselves affirm that the actual spoken, audible words of the Quran are created and temporal in their physical existence (huduth al-kalam al-lafzi). Conversely, if the Mu'tazila were to accept the concept of internal, eternal speech (kalam nafsi) as a divine attribute—something they deny—they would have no grounds to dispute its eternity. The author suggests that the real disagreement is only because the Mu'tazila reject the notion of an eternal attribute of speech, not because they disagree about the createdness of the verbal articulation. This passage underscores that the core issue is the epistemological status of the attribute of speech.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that Ahl al-Sunna believe that every aspect of the Quran, including its physical words and sounds, is uncreated and eternal.
Correction: This passage clarifies that Ahl al-Sunna affirm the createdness of verbal speech (kalam lafzi)—the actual words and sounds of the Quran are created. What they consider eternal is the internal speech (kalam nafsi), the divine attribute of speech that is beyond letters and sounds.

Checkpoint

B) They say it is created.
C) They have no view on it.
D) They say it does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The apparent dispute over the createdness of the Quran is resolved by distinguishing between created verbal speech and eternal internal speech.

The Mu'tazila would agree with Ahl al-Sunna if they accepted the concept of internal speech through a 'decisive proof'. Is your own belief in the Quran's eternity based on a decisive proof, or on blind acceptance? How would you respond if someone asked you for that proof?
?
If the heart of this dispute is merely linguistic, what does that reveal about your own tendency to cling to disagreements? Is there a dispute in your life where you need to step back and ask whether it is real or just a matter of words? What concrete step will you take this week to seek clarity instead of conflict?

Allah's Eternal Attributes: Real, Not Potential
Commentary argues that Allah's attributes of speech and creation are truly realized in pre-eternity, not merely potential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attributes of speech and creation are eternally realized in His essence, and that their delay in manifestation does not imply potentiality or temporality.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are eternally real, not merely potential.

This passage defends the orthodox view that Allah's attributes of speech and creation are truly realized in pre-eternity, even before their objects exist. The commentator refutes the suggestion that Allah was only 'potentially' Creator or Speaker, clarifying the distinction between capability and potentiality and providing a logical proof rooted in the nature of origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice the key terms: mutakallim, khāliq, quwwa, fi‘l.
Pay attention to the parenthetical glosses explaining the meanings.
2
Identify the Two Claims
The passage states that Allah was speaking in pre-eternity but had not spoken to Moses, and that Allah was Creator in pre-eternity but had not yet created. Understand that the attributes are affirmed even before their objects exist.
3
Examine the Refutation of 'Potential'
The commentator rejects the idea that Allah was 'Creator potentially' (bi al-quwwa) because it implies possibility (imkān) and temporality. Understand why potentiality is incompatible with the divine attribute.
The refutation is based on the logical consequence of indeterminacy.
4
Follow the Logical Proof
Understand the argument: whatever goes from potentiality to actuality is originated (ḥādith). Since every possible being is originated, if Allah's attribute were potential, it would be originated – which is false.
This proof relies on the principle that possible beings are contingent and come into existence.
5
Reflect on the Analogy
Compare the two cases: one who is able to write but delays (qādir) vs. one who is a writer potentially (kātib bi al-quwwa). The latter is incapable in the present; the former has real ability. This illustrates that Allah's attribute is truly realized, not potential.
The analogy clarifies the difference between having the power to act and being in a state of potentiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد كان الله تعالى (متكلما) أي في الأزل ولم يكن كلّم موسى عليه السلام) أي والحال أنه لم يكن كلم موسى بل ولا خلق أصل موسى وعيسى. وقد كان الله تعالى خالقا في الأزل ولم يخلق الخلق جملة حالية والمعنى أن الحق كان خالقا قبل خلق الخلق، وفي نسخة: «وكان الله خالقنا قبل أن يخلق الخلق حقيقة»، بمعنى أن هذا النعت فيه محقق لا مجاز، كما قال ابن أبي شريف: إنه كان خالقا بالقوة». فإنه يوهم أنه تحت الإمكان واحتمال الوقوع واللاوقوع في الأزمان، وليس الأمر كذلك، فإنه كان خالقا متحقق الوقوع في وقت أراد فيه الشروع، فتأخر متعلق الكلام والخلق من موسى وسائر الأنام لا يوجب نفي صحة الكلام ونفي تحقق الخلق عن الحق عند العلماء الأعلام؛ لأن كل شيء يكون في القوة ثم يصير إلى الفعل فهو حادث، إذ كل ممكن الوجود حادث كما صرحوا به، وأيضاً فرق واضح وبون لائح بين من هو قادر على الكتابة إلا أنه يؤخرها إلى وقت الإرادة وبين الكاتب بالقوة، حيث إنه عاجز في الحالة الراهنة وتحت الاحتمال في الأزمنة الآتية.
And Allah Almighty was 'speaking' (mutakalliman) meaning in pre-eternity (al-azal) and He had not spoken to Moses, peace be upon him, meaning that He had not yet spoken to Moses, nor had He created the origin of Moses and Jesus. And Allah Almighty was 'creator' (khāliqan) in pre-eternity and had not created the creation—a circumstantial clause (jumla ḥāliyya). The meaning is that the Truth was Creator before creating creation. And in another recension: 'And Allah was our Creator before He created the creation truly (ḥaqīqa),' meaning that this attribute (na't) is realized (muḥaqqaq) in Him, not metaphorical (majāz), as Ibn Abī Sharīf said: 'He was Creator potentially (bi al-quwwa).' That gives the impression that it is under possibility and the possibility of occurrence and non-occurrence in times, but it is not so. He was Creator with a realized occurrence (mutaḥaqqiq al-wuqū') at the time He intended to commence. So the delay of the object of speech and creation from Moses and all people does not necessitate negating the validity of speech and negating the realization of creation from the Truth, according to the leading scholars; because everything that is in potency (quwwa) then becomes actuality (fi'l) is originated (ḥādith), since every possible being is originated as they have affirmed. Also, there is a clear difference and apparent distinction between one who is able to write (qādir ʿalā al-kitāba) but delays it to the time of will, and the writer potentially (al-kātib bi al-quwwa), for he is incapable in the present state and under possibility in future times.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the eternal attributes of Allah, specifically speech (kalām) and creation (khalq). The author discusses the statement that 'Allah was speaking (mutakallim) in pre-eternity (al-azal) but had not spoken to Moses' and 'Allah was Creator (khāliq) in pre-eternity but had not created the creation.' The meaning is that Allah's attribute of being a Speaker and a Creator is eternally realized (muḥaqqaq) and not merely potential. The author refutes an interpretation by Ibn Abī Sharīf that Allah was 'Creator potentially' (khāliq bi al-quwwa). Such an interpretation would imply that the attribute is subject to possibility (imkān) and could either occur or not occur in time, which is not befitting of Allah. Instead, Allah is truly Creator with a realized occurrence (mutaḥaqqiq al-wuqū') at the time He willed to begin creation. The delay in the actual creation of Moses and others does not negate the reality of the attribute. The author provides a logical proof: anything that goes from potentiality (quwwa) to actuality (fi'l) is originated (ḥādith). Since every possible being is originated, the attribute of creation itself cannot be in a state of potentiality before creation because that would imply temporality. He then gives a clear analogy: there is a difference between someone who has the ability to write but delays writing until he wills (qādir ʿalā al-kitāba) and someone who is merely a writer in potentiality (kātib bi al-quwwa). The latter is actually incapable in the present moment and only has a possibility of writing in the future. This distinction shows that Allah's attribute of creation is not merely potential but truly realized, even before the creation exists.

Key Terms
متكلم (speaking)
خالق (creator)
أزل (pre-eternity)
قوة (potentiality)
فعل (actuality)
حدوث (origination)
إمكان (possibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah's creation is not yet actualized, then He is not truly Creator; His attribute must have been 'potential' before creation.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Allah's attribute of creation is truly realized (muḥaqqaq) even before the object exists, and the delay in actual creation does not negate the reality of the attribute. Potentiality would imply originatedness, which is impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it incorrect to say Allah was 'Creator potentially'?
A) Because it implies Allah is incapable
B) Because it suggests possibility of non-occurrence, which is not befitting
C) Because the Quran explicitly denies it
D) Because creation is eternal

Key Takeaway
Allah's attributes of speech and creation are eternally realized in His essence, not merely potential, and their manifestation in time does not imply temporality or deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you commit to listening to His speech this week, not just reading the words, but knowing that they are from the Living One whose speech is eternal?
?
When you look at your own life and see your sins and shortcomings, do you sometimes think that Allah's attribute of creation is 'on hold' until you become worthy? The lesson says He is eternally Creator, truly your Creator even before you existed. Will you submit today to His creation of you exactly as He has determined, without resistance?
?
The analogy distinguishes between one who is able to write but delays, and one who is potentially a writer but actually incapable. In your relationship with Allah, do you treat His forgiveness and mercy as merely potential—something He might give if you earn it? Or do you believe His mercy is already realized, waiting for you to seek it? Will you seek His mercy right now with that certainty?
