God's Eternal Attributes: Speech and Creation in Pre-Eternity
Commentary on God being mutakallim and khaliq in pre-eternity with temporal manifestation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine attributes like speech and creation are eternal in God even though their effects appear in time, and why this does not imply potentiality or change in God.

Core Idea
God's attributes are eternal; their temporal manifestation does not make the attributes temporal.

This lesson addresses a subtle but critical point in Islamic theology: how can God be described as 'speaking' and 'creating' before He actually spoke to Moses or created the universe? The commentary you will read explains that these attributes are real and eternal in God, while their objects are temporal. This distinction safeguards God's transcendence and perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the two attributes discussed: speech and creation.
2
Focus on key terms
Identify the terms for 'speaking' (متكلّم) and 'creator' (خالق) and how they are qualified by 'in pre-eternity' (في الأزل). Understand that the attribute exists even before the object.
The term 'القوة والفعل' (potentiality and actuality) is central to the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation of potentiality
The author rejects the idea that God being 'creator in pre-eternity' means He is merely potentially a creator. Instead, His power is fully realized but He chooses the time of action.
Compare the 'writer in potentiality' analogy to grasp the difference.
4
Reflect on al-Tahawi's summary
Read al-Tahawi's quote which concludes that God deserves the name Creator before creation, just as He deserves the name Life-Giver before giving life.
This reinforces that attributes are intrinsic, not acquired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد كان الله تعالى (متكلما) أي في الأزل ولم يكن كلّم موسى عليه السلام) أي والحال أنه لم يكن كلم موسى بل ولا خلق أصل موسى وعيسى. وقد كان الله تعالى خالقا في الأزل ولم يخلق الخلق جملة حالية والمعنى أن الحق كان خالقا قبل خلق الخلق، وفي نسخة: «وكان الله خالقنا قبل أن يخلق الخلق حقيقة»، بمعنى أن هذا النعت فيه محقق لا مجاز، كما قال ابن أبي شريف: إنه كان خالقا بالقوة». فإنه يوهم أنه تحت الإمكان واحتمال الوقوع واللاوقوع في الأزمان، وليس الأمر كذلك، فإنه كان خالقا متحقق الوقوع في وقت أراد فيه الشروع، فتأخر متعلق الكلام والخلق من موسى وسائر الأنام لا يوجب نفي صحة الكلام ونفي تحقق الخلق عن الحق عند العلماء الأعلام؛ لأن كل شيء يكون في القوة ثم يصير إلى الفعل فهو حادث، إذ كل ممكن الوجود حادث كما صرحوا به، وأيضاً فرق واضح وبون لائح بين من هو قادر على الكتابة إلا أنه يؤخرها إلى وقت الإرادة وبين الكاتب بالقوة، حيث إنه عاجز في الحالة الراهنة وتحت الاحتمال في الأزمنة الآتية. والحاصل أنه سبحانه كما قال الطحاوي رحمه الله: ليس منذ خلق الخلق استفاد اسم الخالق، ولا بإحداثه البرية استفاد اسم الباري فله معنى الربوبية ولا مربوب ومعنى الخالقية ولا مخلوق الموتى بعد ما أحي استحق هذا الاسم قبل إحيائهم، وكذلك استحق اسم الخالق قبل إنشائهم، ذلك بأنه على كل شيء قدير، وإليه كل شيء فقير، وكل أمر عليه يسير.
And Allah Almighty was (speaking) i.e. in pre-eternity, and He had not spoken to Moses, peace be upon him, i.e. while He had not spoken to Moses, nor even created the origin of Moses and Jesus. And Allah Almighty was creator in pre-eternity and had not created the creation — a circumstantial clause, meaning that the Truth was creator before the creation of creatures. And in a manuscript version: 'And Allah was our creator before He created the creation truly,' meaning that this attribute is realized in Him, not metaphorical, as Ibn Abi Sharif said: 'He was creator in potentiality.' For it suggests that it is under possibility and probability of occurrence and non-occurrence in times, but it is not so; rather He was a creator with realized occurrence at the time He willed to begin. So the delay of the object of speech and creation of Moses and all other people does not necessitate the negation of the soundness of speech nor the negation of the realization of creation from the Truth according to the great scholars; because everything that is in potentiality and then becomes actual is originated, since every possible existence is originated as they stated. Also there is a clear difference and evident distinction between one who is able to write but delays it to a time of will and the writer in potentiality, as he is incapable in the present state and under probability in future times. And the conclusion is that He, glorified, as al-Tahawi, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'It is not that since He created creation He acquired the name Creator, nor by bringing the creatures into existence did He acquire the name Maker; for He has the meaning of Lordship without a lorded, and the meaning of Creatorship without a created. And just as He, after He gave life to the dead, deserved this name before giving them life, so too He deserved the name Creator before their creation. That is because He is over all things powerful, and to Him everything is needy, and every matter is easy for Him.'
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative commentary on two fundamental divine attributes: speech (kalam) and creation (khalq) in their relation to pre-eternity. The author begins by affirming that God was 'mutakallim' (speaking) in pre-eternity, even though He had not yet spoken to Moses or created Moses and Jesus. The key point is that the attribute exists eternally; its temporal manifestation does not imply that the attribute itself is temporal. Similarly, God was 'khaliq' (creator) in pre-eternity even before creating anything. The author refutes a potential misunderstanding: this does not mean God was a mere 'potential' creator like a person who has the ability to write but is currently unable. Rather, God's power is fully realized in pre-eternity, but He chooses to bring about the act at a specific time. The delay of the object (maf'ul) does not negate the perfection of the attribute. The author quotes al-Tahawi to reinforce that God did not acquire the name 'Creator' upon creating; He was always worthy of it. The essence of the argument is that temporal objects of eternal attributes do not affect the eternity of the attributes themselves.

Key Terms
متكلّم
خالق
الأزل
القوة والفعل
الحادث
الممكن الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's being 'creator in pre-eternity' implies He is a mere potential creator like a human who can but has not yet acted.
Correction: God's creatorhood is fully realized in pre-eternity; He is not a potential creator but one whose power is complete and who chooses the time of action.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does God being 'khaliq' in pre-eternity not mean He is a merely potential creator?
A) Because potentiality implies incapability in the present, but God's power is fully realized.
B) Because potential and actual are the same for God.
C) Because creation is eternal like the attribute.
D) Because God created the universe in pre-eternity.

Key Takeaway
God’s attributes like speech and creation are eternal and fully realized in His essence; their temporal objects do not diminish their eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a long delay in an answer to your dua or in seeing God's intervention in your life, do you ever doubt that He is fully able or willing? How should the truth that God's attributes were perfect in pre-eternity before any action change how you wait?
?
God was Creator before the universe existed, Speaker before He spoke to any prophet. If His perfect attributes are not dependent on their objects, what does that say about your own worth or need to perform to earn His attention? Does this humble you or free you?
?
The passage warns that confusing God's eternal power with human 'potential' diminishes Him. Where in your heart have you subconsciously compared God's slowness to human weakness? What will you do today to replace that thought with the certainty that His delay is not deficiency?

God's Eternal Speech and His Speaking to Moses
Clarifying that God's speech to Moses is the expression of an eternal attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's speech to Moses is the manifestation of an eternal attribute, not a new, created speech, and how the commentator clarifies this through textual variants and analogies.

Core Idea
God's speech to Moses was with His eternal speech, not a new creation.

This passage from a classical commentary on Fiqh al-Akbar addresses a potential misunderstanding: did God's speaking to Moses imply a temporal act? The commentator clarifies that God's speech is an eternal attribute, and His speaking to Moses was the expression of that eternal speech in time. Through manuscript variants and an analogy to the Preserved Tablet, the passage defends the doctrine of divine speech as uncreated.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the manuscript variants enclosed in parentheses.
2
Identify the manuscript variants
The passage mentions three variants: 'And Allah was speaking' placed after 'creator', 'He is an attribute for Him', and 'He is from His attributes'. Understand how each emphasizes the eternality of speech.
3
Understand the parenthetical clause about creation
The clause about creation is parenthetical to highlight that Moses' creation is an event among creation, questioning how his status relates to the speech of God.
This refutes any claim that Moses' merit caused God's speech.
4
Grasp the interpretation of 'When He spoke to Moses'
The commentator explains that God spoke to Moses with His speech that is eternal (qadima). The various manuscript readings all affirm that speech is an eternal attribute.
The key phrase is 'kalamahu alladhi huwa qadima' (His speech which is eternal).
5
Reflect on the analogy of the Preserved Tablet
The passage compares God's speaking to Moses to inscribing words in the Preserved Tablet—an eternal meaning expressed in a temporal medium.
This illustrates how an eternal attribute can manifest without becoming temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نسخة: «وقد كان الله متكلما» متأخر عن قوله: «وقد كان الله تعالى خالقا»، وعلى كل تقدير فالجملة المتعلقة بالخلق اعتراضية للإشعار بأن خلق موسى حادث في أثناء خلق الأنام، فكيف مقامه في مرام الكلام؟ (فلما كلّم) أي الله كما في نسخة (موسى) والمعنى أراد تكليمه إياه كلمه بكلامه الذي هو أي قديمة، وفي نسخة: «هو صفة له وفي نسخة: «هو من صفاته» (في الأزل) يعني أنه كلمه بمضمون كلامه القديم الأزلي الأقدس كما نقش الكلمات الدالة عليه في اللوح المحفوظ الأنفس
And in a manuscript version: 'And Allah was speaking' is deferred after His saying: 'And Allah Almighty was creator.' In any case, the sentence related to creation is parenthetical to indicate that the creation of Moses is an event during the creation of other people, so how could his station be in the matter of speech? (When He spoke) i.e. Allah, as in a manuscript version, (to Moses) and the meaning is: He willed to speak to him, He spoke to him with His speech which is, i.e. eternal. And in a manuscript version: 'He is an attribute for Him' and in a manuscript version: 'He is from His attributes' (in pre-eternity), meaning that He spoke to him with the content of His ancient, pre-eternal, most holy speech, just as He inscribed the indicative words concerning it in the illuminated Preserved Tablet.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a textual and theological clarification of how God's speech to Moses relates to the eternal attribute. The author notes a variant manuscript order where the statement 'And God was speaking' appears after 'God was creator'. He explains that the clause about creation is parenthetical (i'tiradiyya) to highlight that Moses' creation occurs amid other creations, questioning how his status could be relevant to the speech of God. The main point is the interpretation of 'When He spoke to Moses': God spoke to Moses with His speech which is eternal (qadima). The author reviews different manuscript readings that emphasize this speech is an attribute of God and is in pre-eternity. The concluding simile compares this eternal speech to words inscribed in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz), indicating that the manifestation of God's speech to Moses is like the inscription of eternal meanings in a temporal medium. Thus, the actual event of speaking to Moses is the expression of an eternal attribute, not a new, created speech.

كلام قديم
اللوح المحفوظ
اعتراضية
المضمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's speaking to Moses implies His speech is temporal or originated with the event.
Correction: God's speech is an eternal attribute; His speaking to Moses was the manifestation of that eternal speech, not a new creation.

Checkpoint
How does the passage explain God's speaking to Moses in relation to the attribute of speech?
A) God created a new speech for Moses.
B) God spoke to Moses with His eternal speech.
C) God's speech to Moses was metaphorical.
D) Moses heard God's speech through a created medium.

Key Takeaway
God's speech to Moses was the manifestation of His eternal, uncreated speech, not a temporal event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Allah’s Eternal Speech and Created Words
Explains how God’s eternal speech is heard through created sounds without temporality in the attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah's speech is an eternal attribute while the words heard by Moses were created sounds that serve as evidences of that eternal attribute.

Core Idea
Allah's eternal speech is heard through created words; the attribute is uncreated, the sounds are created.

This passage addresses a subtle theological point: how can Allah's eternal speech be heard by a created being without making the attribute itself temporal? The author explains that the words heard are created and originate in time, but they point to the real, eternal attribute of speech. This distinction protects Allah's transcendence while affirming the reality of prophetic revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the parenthetical remarks about manuscript variants.
2
Identify the key distinction
Look for the distinction between Allah’s eternal attribute (speech) and its temporal objects (the actual sounds). The passage also differentiates the attribute of creating from its objects.
3
Understand the rational proof
The passage states that accidents (ḥawādith) cannot inhere in Allah’s essence. Follow why this leads to the conclusion that His speech is eternal.
This is based on both religious and rational proofs.
4
Analyze the parenthetical about creation
The author inserts a clause about creation to highlight that Moses was created later. Consider why this is relevant to the discussion of speech.
It emphasizes that Moses’ hearing is temporal.
5
Observe the role of the Preserved Tablet
The author compares the eternal speech to words inscribed in the Preserved Tablet. The words Moses heard are created, but they are evidences (adilla) of the real eternal attribute.
This illustrates how the eternal manifests through temporal signs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من جملة ما قالوا في قوله: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ، إنه إما أريد به المبالغة، أي ليس لمثله مثل لو فرض المثل كيف ولا مثل له وقد علمت بالأدلة الشرعية والعقلية استحالة قيام الحوادث بذات الله الأزلية الأبدية، فكلامه قديم وكذا صفة خلقه، وأما متعلقاتهما فحادثة في وقت تعلق الإرادة بوقوعها. وفي نسخة: «وقد كان الله متكلما» متأخر عن قوله: «وقد كان الله تعالى خالقا»، وعلى كل تقدير فالجملة المتعلقة بالخلق اعتراضية للإشعار بأن خلق موسى حادث في أثناء خلق الأنام، فكيف مقامه في مرام الكلام؟ (فلما كلّم) أي الله كما في نسخة (موسى) والمعنى أراد تكليمه إياه كلمه بكلامه الذي هو أي قديمة، وفي نسخة: «هو صفة له وفي نسخة: «هو من صفاته» (في الأزل) يعني أنه كلمه بمضمون كلامه القديم الأزلي الأقدس كما نقش الكلمات الدالة عليه في اللوح المحفوظ الأنفس قبل خلق السموات والأرض والأنفس، فكلمه على وفق تلك الكلمات المسطورة، فتلك الكلمات المزبورة والكلمات التي سمعها موسى عليه السلام من الشجرة المشهورة حادثة مخلوقة، إلا أنها أدلة كلامه الذي هو صفته الأزلية الحقيقية.
Then among what they said concerning His saying: “There is nothing like unto Him” is that it is intended for emphasis, i.e., there is no likeness to His likeness even if a likeness were supposed—how could there be a likeness when He has no likeness? And it is known by both religious and rational proofs the impossibility of accidents (al-ḥawādith) subsisting in the essence of Allah, the eternal and everlasting. Thus His speech is eternal, and likewise the attribute of His creating. As for their objects, they are originated at the time the will attaches to their occurrence. In one copy: “And Allah was speaking” is delayed after His saying: “And Allah, exalted be He, was Creator.” In any case, the clause related to creation is parenthetical to indicate that the creation of Moses occurred in the midst of the creation of mankind; so what is his station in the matter of speech? (When He spoke) i.e., Allah as in one copy (to Moses) means He intended to speak to him; He spoke to him with His speech which is—i.e., eternal, and in one copy: “it is an attribute of His” and in one copy: “it is from His attributes” (in pre-eternity) meaning He spoke to him with the content of His pre-eternal, most holy speech, just as He inscribed the words indicating it in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawḥ al-maḥfūẓ) and in the souls before the creation of the heavens, the earth, and the souls. So He spoke to him in accordance with those inscribed words. Thus those mentioned words and the words that Moses, peace be upon him, heard from the famous tree are originated and created, but they are evidences of His speech which is His real, eternal attribute.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation of how Allah’s speech (kalām) is an eternal attribute while the actual words heard by prophets are created. The author begins by noting that some interpret “laysa kamithlihi shay’” as an intensive negation, meaning there is absolutely no likeness. He then asserts that it is established by both revelation and reason that temporal events (ḥawādith) cannot inhere in Allah’s eternal essence. Hence Allah’s speech is eternal, as is His attribute of creating (takwīn), while the things created are temporal. The author mentions variant manuscript readings about Allah being “mutakallim” and “khāliq”. The clause about creation is parenthetical to stress that Moses’ creation was later. The key point: when Allah spoke to Moses, He spoke with His eternal speech, but the audible words Moses heard were created sounds that indicate that eternal, uncreated attribute. These created words are evidence (dalīl) of the real eternal attribute.

Refuting Two Errors on Divine Speech
Refutes two errors from Abū Ḥanīfa's statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two errors about divine speech that Abū Ḥanīfa’s statement refutes: the view that speech is an unhearable meaning and the view that speech is originated in time.

Core Idea
Abū Ḥanīfa’s words silence two false claims about God’s speech.

The great imam Abū Ḥanīfa once said that when God spoke to Moses, He spoke with His speech which is from His attributes. This short statement carries deep theological weight, refuting two major misunderstandings. In this lesson, we explore those refutations as explained by the commentator on 'Aqīdat al-Ṭaḥāwī.

Guided Path
1
Read Abū Ḥanīfa's Statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the commentator introduces the imam's words.
The statement is: 'When He spoke to Moses, He spoke to him with His speech which is from His attributes.'
2
Identify the First Error Refuted
The commentator explains that the statement refutes those who say speech is a single meaning in the self that cannot be heard, and that sound is created. This is attributed to some companions and al-Māturīdī.
This error denies that God's speech can be heard directly.
3
Identify the Second Error Refuted
The phrase 'which is from His attributes' refutes those who say the attribute of speech occurred to God after He was not speaking.
This error denies the eternality of the attribute of speech.
4
Understand the Quranic Evidence
The verse 'And when Moses came to Our appointed meeting and his Lord spoke to him' is the basis for understanding that God spoke at a specific time without speaking eternally.
This shows the balance between eternal attribute and temporal event.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشارح عقيدة الطحاوي: قول الإمام الأعظم: «فلما كلم موسى كلمه بكلامه الذي هو من صفاته». يعلم أنه حين جاء كلمه لا أنه لم يزل ولا يزال أزلاً وأبداً يقول ياموسى كما ذلك من قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَمَّا جَاءَ مُوسَى لِمِيقَاتِنَاوَ كَلَّمَهُ رَبُّهُ ﴾ ففهم منه الرد على من يقول من أصحابه إنه معنى واحد قائم بالنفس لا يتصور أن يسمع، وإنما يخلق الله الصوت في الهواء كما قاله أبو منصور الماتريدي. وقول الإمام الأعظم: «الذي هو من صفاته» رد على من يقول إنه حدث له وصف الكلام بعد أن لم يكن متكلما. وبالجملة فكل ما يحتج به المعتزلة مما يدل على كلام متعلق بمشيئته وقدرته وأنه متكلم إذا شاء وأنه يتكلم شيئا بعد شيء فهو حق يجب قبوله، وما يقول به من يقول : إن كلام الله قائم بذاته وأنه صفة له والصفة لا تقوم إلا بالموصوف فهو حق يجب قبوله والقول به، فيجب الأخذ بما في قول كل من الطائفتين من الصواب والعدول عما يردّه الشرع والعقل من قول كل منهما، وهذا فصل الخطاب. وقد قال صلى الله عليه وسلم: ((أعوذ بكلمات الله )) وهو عليه الصلاة والسلام لم يتعوذ بمخلوق، بل هو كقوله: ((أعوذ برضاك)) وقوله : ((أعوذ بعزة الله وقدرته)). وكثير من متأخري الحنفية على أنه معنى واحد والتعدد والتكثر والتجزي والتبعض حاصل.
And the commentator on ‘Aqīdat al-Ṭaḥāwī said: The statement of the Supreme Imām (Abū Ḥanīfa): “When He spoke to Moses, He spoke to him with His speech which is from His attributes.” It is understood that He spoke to him when he came, not that He ceaselessly says ‘O Moses’ eternally and forever, just as that is from His saying: “And when Moses came to Our appointed meeting and his Lord spoke to him.” So from this is understood the refutation of those among his companions who say that it is a single meaning subsisting in the self that cannot be heard, and that Allah creates sound in the air as Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī said. And the statement of the Supreme Imām: “which is from His attributes” is a refutation of those who say that the attribute of speech occurred to Him after He was not speaking. In summary, everything with which the Mu‘tazila argue, indicating speech connected to His will and power, and that He speaks when He wills and speaks thing after thing—it is true and must be accepted. And what is said by those who say that Allah’s speech subsists in His essence and is an attribute of His, and the attribute subsists only in the one described—it is true and must be accepted and stated. So one must take from the statement of each of the two groups what is correct and turn away from what is rejected by revelation and reason from each of their statements. This is the decisive word. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: “I seek refuge in the words of Allah.” He, peace be upon him, did not seek refuge in a created thing; rather it is like his saying: “I seek refuge in Your pleasure” and his saying: “I seek refuge in the might and power of Allah.” And many of the later Ḥanafīs hold that it (i.e., the speech) is one meaning, and multiplicity, increase, division, and partiality occur.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a reconciliation (talfīq) between two major theological positions on divine speech. The author first explains that Abū Ḥanīfa’s statement about God speaking to Moses with His speech (which is one of His attributes) refutes two errors: first, the view that speech is a single, unhearable meaning (a position attributed to some of Abū Ḥanīfa’s followers and to al-Māturīdī); second, the view that the attribute of speech is originated in time (the Mu‘tazilī position). The author then declares that both sides have truth: the Mu‘tazila are correct that speech is connected to will and occurs in parts, while the Ash‘arī/Māturīdī position is correct that speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in God’s essence. One should accept the sound elements from each and reject what contradicts revelation and reason. The author supports this with the hadith of seeking refuge in the words of Allah, arguing that since refuge is not sought in created things, the words must be uncreated. Finally, he notes that later Ḥanafī scholars hold the speech to be one meaning, while multiplicity occurs in its expression.

Key Terms
معنى واحد
قيام الكلام بالذات
الماتريدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Abū Ḥanīfa’s phrase “which is from His attributes” specifically refute?
A) The view that speech is a single meaning.
B) The view that speech is originated in time.
C) The view that speech is created sound.
D) The view that speech is multiple meanings.

Key Takeaway
Abū Ḥanīfa’s statement refutes the errors that speech is an unhearable meaning and that speech is originated for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly believe you are hearing God's eternal speech, or do you tend to think of it as a created thing? If the latter, what does that say about your reverence for His words?
?
Abū Ḥanīfa's statement refutes the idea that God's speech is only a mental concept that cannot be heard. Have you ever reduced God's speech to abstract ideas, distancing yourself from the reality that He spoke audibly to prophets? How can you change that?

Reconciling the Two Views on Divine Speech
Harmonizes Mu‘tazilī and Ash‘arī views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the balanced method of reconciling the Mu‘tazilī and Ash‘arī positions on divine speech by accepting the sound elements from each.

Core Idea
The truth lies in taking what is correct from both sides and rejecting what contradicts revelation and reason.

After refuting errors, the commentator offers a masterful reconciliation. He affirms that both the Mu‘tazilī and Ash‘arī positions contain truth, and the decisive approach is to take the sound parts from each. This lesson explores that reconciliation and its proof from the hadith of seeking refuge in the words of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Reconciliation Passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the phrase 'this is the decisive word' (faṣl al-khiṭāb).
2
Identify the Accepted Mu‘tazilī View
The Mu‘tazila argue that speech is connected to will and power, and God speaks when He wills and speaks sequentially. This is accepted.
3
Identify the Accepted Ash‘arī View
The Ash‘arīs argue that speech subsists in God's essence and is an attribute that only subsists in the described. This is also accepted.
4
Understand the Principle of Selection
The author says we must take what is correct from each and turn away from what contradicts revelation and reason.
This is the golden mean.
5
Examine the Hadith and Later Ḥanafī View
The Prophet sought refuge in the words of Allah, not in a created thing. Later Ḥanafīs hold that speech is one meaning with multiplicity in expression.
The hadith supports uncreatedness.

Created Words as Evidence of Eternal Speech
Sanusi distinguishes between created audible words and the eternal attribute of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the words heard by Prophet Musa were created yet serve as evidence of Allah's eternal, uncreated attribute of speech.

Core Idea
Allah's eternal speech is evidenced, not exhausted, by created words.

This lesson addresses a crucial theological nuance: while the words that Musa heard from the tree were temporally created, they are not the entire reality of Allah's speech. Rather, they point to and are evidence of His true eternal attribute. The author makes a clear distinction between the created manifestation and the eternal quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic text and the literal translation carefully. Notice the contrast between 'created' and 'eternal attribute'.
Pay attention to the word 'illa' (except) which introduces the qualification.
2
Understand the Key Terms
Identify the two key terms: حادثة مخلوقة (temporally originated, created) and صفته الأزلية الحقيقية (His true eternal attribute). These terms capture the distinction.
3
Reflect on the Theological Point
Ask yourself: How can created words be evidence of an eternal attribute? The answer lies in the author's qualification: they are not the attribute itself but point to it.
This guards against the misconception that Allah's speech is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبل خلق السموات والأرض، والأنفس، فكلمه على وفق تلك الكلمات المسطورة، فتلك الكلمات المزبورة والكلمات التي سمعها موسى عليه السلام من الشجرة المشهورة حادثة مخلوقة، إلا أنها أدلة كلامه الذي هو صفته الأزلية الحقيقية.
Before the creation of the heavens and the earth and the souls, He spoke to him according to those inscribed words, so those mentioned words and the words that Moses, peace be upon him, heard from the famous tree are temporally originated, created, except that they are evidences of His speech which is His true eternal attribute.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a concise theological statement. It begins by referring to a time before creation, indicating the pre-existence of Allah's speech. 'Those inscribed words' likely refer to the eternal speech. The words that Musa heard from the burning bush are described as 'hadithah makhluqah' (temporally originated, created). However, the author immediately qualifies: 'illa annaha adillatu kalamihi' (except that they are evidences of His speech). This guards against the misunderstanding that the created words constitute the entire speech of Allah. Rather, they point to and are evidence of His true, eternal attribute of speech (sifatihi al-azaliyyah al-haqiqiyyah). Thus, the passage distinguishes between the created manifestation and the eternal attribute.

Key Terms
حادثة مخلوقة
صفته الأزلية الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because the words were created, Allah's speech is created.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the created words are evidence of the eternal speech, not the speech itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the words heard by Musa and Allah's eternal speech?
A) They are identical to His eternal speech.
B) They are created but evidence of His eternal speech.
C) They are the only form of His speech.
D) They are not related.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech as an attribute is eternal and uncreated, while any audible or written word that we encounter is a created evidence of that attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the Quran, do you treat it merely as a historical text created in time, or do you revere it as an evidence of Allah's eternal speech? How does this distinction change your relationship with the words you recite?
?
The words Musa heard from the tree were created, yet they pointed to something eternal. What 'created' things in your life—people, events, or experiences—are you treating as ultimate rather than as pointers to Allah's eternal attributes? What would it mean for you to see them as signs instead?
?
If Allah's speech is eternal and uncreated, how does that challenge your assumption that you can fully grasp or control His message through your own interpretations? Will you humble yourself before the Quran as a reflection of the eternal, not just a human composition?

Reconciling Two Positions on Divine Speech: The Decisive Word
Commentator explains Imam Abu Hanifa's statement, refutes two errors, reconciles two correct positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator reconciles the Mu'tazilite and Ahl al-Sunna positions on divine speech, accepting truth from both and rejecting error.

Core Idea
Both sides have truth; the decisive word is to take the right from each.

In this passage, the commentator on the Tahawi creed unpacks a statement of Imam Abu Hanifa about Allah speaking to Moses. He uses it to refute two erroneous positions and then offers a remarkable reconciliation between the Mu'tazilite emphasis on speech as an act of will and the Sunni emphasis on speech as an eternal attribute. This lesson walks through that process.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument flow.
Notice the commentator quotes Imam Abu Hanifa and then expands.
2
Identify the two errors refuted
The commentator refutes those who say speech is one meaning not heard (Maturidi) and those who say speech occurred after not being. Locate these in the passage.
3
Understand the two correct positions
The Mu'tazila correctly hold that speech is an act of will; Ahl al-Sunna correctly hold that speech is an eternal attribute. Both have truth.
The passage says each is 'truth that must be accepted'.
4
Grasp the reconciliation principle
The decisive word (fasl al-khitab) is to take the truth from both sides and reject what contradicts reason and revelation. This is the method.
5
Reflect on the broader method
Consider how this approach—taking valid elements from differing views—can be applied in other theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشارح عقيدة الطحاوي: قول الإمام الأعظم: «فلما كلم موسى كلمه بكلامه الذي هو من صفاته». يعلم أنه حين جاء كلمه لا أنه لم يزل ولا يزال أزلاً وأبداً يقول ياموسى كما ذلك من قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَمَّا جَاءَ مُوسَى لِمِيقَاتِنَا وَكَلَّمَهُ رَبُّهُ ﴾ [الاعراف:١٤٣] ففهم منه الرد على من يقول من أصحابه إنه معنى واحد قائم بالنفس لا يتصور أن يسمع، وإنما يخلق الله الصوت في الهواء كما قاله أبو منصور الماتريدي. وقول الإمام الأعظم: «الذي هو من صفاته» رد على من يقول إنه حدث له وصف الكلام بعد أن لم يكن متكلما. وبالجملة فكل ما يحتج به المعتزلة مما يدل على كلام متعلق بمشيئته وقدرته وأنه متكلم إذا شاء وأنه يتكلم شيئا بعد شيء فهو حق يجب قبوله، وما يقول به من يقول : إن كلام الله قائم بذاته وأنه صفة له والصفة لا تقوم إلا بالموصوف فهو حق يجب قبوله والقول به، فيجب الأخذ بما في قول كل من الطائفتين من الصواب والعدول عما يردّه الشرع والعقل من قول كل منهما، وهذا فصل الخطاب.
And the commentator on the Tahawi creed said: The saying of the Greatest Imam: 'When He spoke to Moses, He spoke to him with His speech which is of His attributes.' It is known that He spoke to him at the time of coming, not that He has not ceased and will not cease eternally and forever saying 'O Moses' as that is from His statement: {And when Moses came to Our appointed time and his Lord spoke to him} [Al-A'raf: 143]. From this is understood the refutation of those among his followers who say it is one meaning subsisting in the self that cannot be conceived to be heard, but rather Allah creates sound in the air as Abu Mansur al-Maturidi said. And the saying of the Greatest Imam: 'which is of His attributes' is a refutation of those who say that the attribute of speech occurred for Him after He had not been speaking. And in summary: everything that the Mu'tazila argue for that indicates speech connected to His will and power, and that He speaks when He wills and that He speaks thing after thing, is truth that must be accepted; and what is said by those who say that the speech of Allah subsists in His essence and that it is an attribute for Him, and the attribute subsists only in the described, is truth that must be accepted and said. So one must take the correct part from the statement of each of the two groups and avoid what the law and reason reject from the statement of each of them. This is the decisive word.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on a statement of Imam Abu Hanifa (the 'Greatest Imam') regarding Musa hearing Allah's speech. The commentator (the author of the Tahawi creed commentary) starts by quoting Imam Abu Hanifa: 'When He spoke to Moses, He spoke to him with His speech which is of His attributes.' This statement is then analyzed. First, it indicates that the speaking occurred at the specific moment of the encounter (the coming), not that it was a perpetual, eternal saying 'O Moses' — this is derived from the Quranic verse (7:143) which specifies 'when Moses came'. This refutes the position of some Hanafis (like Abu Mansur al-Maturidi) who held that Allah's speech is one meaning subsisting in the self that cannot be heard, and that what Musa heard was a created sound. The second part of Imam's statement — 'which is of His attributes' — refutes those who say that the attribute of speech came to be in Allah after He had not been speaking (i.e., that speech is an accident). The commentator then gives a comprehensive reconciliation: each of the two groups — the Mu'tazila (who emphasize speech as an act of will and power) and the Ahl al-Sunna (who emphasize speech as an eternal attribute subsisting in the essence) — has aspects of truth. One must accept the correct parts from both and reject what is contrary to revelation and reason. This is presented as the definitive resolution ('fasl al-khitab').

Key Terms
كلام متعلق بمشيئته وقدرته
كلام الله قائم بذاته
معنى واحد قائم بالنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite and Ahl al-Sunna positions on divine speech are completely incompatible and one must choose one side entirely.
Correction: The passage shows that each position contains valid truths—Mu'tazila correctly highlight speech as an act of will, Ahl al-Sunna correctly highlight speech as an eternal attribute—and the correct stance is to accept both truths while rejecting what conflicts with revelation and reason.

Checkpoint
What is the 'decisive word' (fasl al-khitab) regarding the two positions on divine speech?
A) Only the Mu'tazilite position is correct.
B) Only the Ahl al-Sunna position is correct.
C) One must accept the truth from both and reject the error from both.
D) Both positions are entirely false.

Key Takeaway
The decisive word is to accept the truth from both Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna on divine speech and reject what contradicts reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's speech, do you find yourself more drawn to the idea of it being an eternal attribute or an act of His will? Which of these makes you feel closer to Him, and are you willing to accept the truth of the other perspective even if it feels less intuitive?
?
The commentator says we must 'avoid what the law and reason reject from each group.' Is there a theological position you hold that might be influenced by a group bias rather than clear evidence? Will you honestly examine that today?
?
Both the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna had elements of truth, but they argued as if they were the whole truth. Where in your own faith journey have you clung to a partial truth as if it were complete, and what would it cost you to humbly accept the full picture?

The Prophet's Refuge in Allah's Words: Proof of Uncreatedness
The author argues from the hadith that Allah's words are uncreated, and refutes the metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of seeking refuge in Allah's words proves they are uncreated, and why the metaphorical interpretation of divine speech leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
The Prophet sought refuge only in the Uncreated; thus Allah's words are uncreated.

This passage presents a powerful proof for the uncreated nature of Allah's words based on the Prophet's own practice. The Prophet Muhammad used the phrase 'I seek refuge in the words of Allah,' and since he would not seek refuge in something created, the words must be uncreated. The passage also addresses and refutes a later theological position that treats the expressions of revelation as merely metaphorical.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and argument
Read the hadith 'I seek refuge in the words of Allah' and the argument that the Prophet would not seek refuge in a created thing. Understand the analogy with seeking refuge in pleasure and might.
2
Understand the later Hanafi position
Read about the later Hanafi view that divine speech is one meaning, but the expressions are created and metaphorically called Allah's speech.
This is the view the author refutes.
3
Identify the refutation
Find the author's refutation by absurd consequence: if expressions are metaphorical and meaning is one, then all verses would have identical meaning, which is false.
Notice the examples: command against fornication vs. command to pray, Surah al-Ikhlas vs. Surah al-Masad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال صلى الله عليه وسلم: ((أعوذ بكلمات الله )). (۱) وهو عليه الصلاة والسلام لم يتعوذ بمخلوق، بل هو كقوله: ((أعوذ برضاك)) . (٢) وقوله : ((أعوذ بعزة الله وقدرته)) . (۳) وكثير من متأخري الحنفية على أنه معنى واحد التعدد والتكثر والتجزي والتبعض حاصل في الدلالات لا في المدلول وهذه العبارات مخلوقة، وسميت كلام الله لدلالتها عليه وتأديته، فإن عبّر بالعربية فهو قرآن، وإن عبّر بالعبرانية فهو توراة فاختلفت العبارات لا الكلام، قالوا وتسمى هذه العبارات كلام الله مجازا، وهذا كلام فاسد، فإن لازمه أن معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْرَبُوا الزَّنّى﴾ [الإسراء: ٣٢] هو معنى قوله: ﴿وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ﴾ [البقرة: ٤٣]، ومعنى آية الكرسي هو معنى آية المداينة، ومعنى سورة الإخلاص هو معنى سورة تبت يدا [تبت: ١]، ثم قال: تَبَّتْ
And the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'I seek refuge in the words of Allah.' And he, peace and blessings be upon him, did not seek refuge in a created thing, but rather it is like his saying: 'I seek refuge in Your pleasure,' and his saying: 'I seek refuge in the might of Allah and His power.' And many of the later Hanafis hold that it is one meaning, and multiplicity, repetition, division, and partitioning occur in the indications, not in the indicated. And these expressions are created, and they are called 'the speech of Allah' because they indicate it and convey it. For if expressed in Arabic, it is the Qur'an, and if expressed in Hebrew, it is the Torah. So the expressions differ, not the speech. They said: These expressions are called the speech of Allah metaphorically. And this is corrupt speech, for its necessary consequence is that the meaning of His statement: 'And do not approach fornication' is the meaning of His statement: 'And establish prayer,' and the meaning of the Verse of the Throne is the meaning of the Verse of Debt, and the meaning of Surah al-Ikhlas is the meaning of Surah Tabbat Yada [Tabat: 1], then He said: 'Perish.'
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the uncreated nature of Allah's words using a hadith. The Prophet Muhammad said 'I seek refuge in the words of Allah.' The argument is: the Prophet would not seek refuge in something created (makhluq), because seeking refuge implies a source of protection that is greater than the creature. Just as he sought refuge in Allah's pleasure and in His might and power—which are uncreated attributes—so too, 'the words of Allah' must be uncreated. The passage then describes the position of later Hanafis: the divine speech itself is one meaning, but the expressions (the Arabic Qur'an, Hebrew Torah, etc.) are created. They are called 'speech of Allah' metaphorically because they indicate and convey the eternal speech. The author refutes this position by showing its absurd logical consequence: if 'speech' is metaphorical and the meaning is one, then all scriptures would have exactly the same meaning. For example, the command 'Do not approach fornication' would have the same meaning as 'Establish prayer,' which is obviously false. Similarly, the meaning of Surah al-Ikhlas (about tawhid) would be the same as Surah al-Masad (which curses Abu Lahab). The absurdity shows that the expressions are not merely metaphorical; they are truly the speech of Allah.

Key Terms
أعوذ بكلمات الله
الدلالات
المدلول
كلام الله مجازا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The written or recited Qur'an is only a metaphorical representation of Allah's eternal speech, not actually His speech.
Correction: The author shows that if this were true, all verses would have the same meaning, which is absurd. Therefore, the recited Qur'an is truly the uncreated speech of Allah.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does the author identify if the expressions are only metaphorically called Allah's speech?
A) The Qur'an would be considered created.
B) All verses would have the same meaning.
C) The Torah would be invalid.
D) The Prophet would not have used the phrase.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's seeking refuge in Allah's words proves they are uncreated, and the metaphorical interpretation is invalid because it leads to absurd contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an in prayer, do you truly feel that you are speaking the very words of Allah, or do you sometimes treat it as a mere book of guidance? How would your recitation change if you fully believed that every letter is uncreated and from Him?
?
The Prophet sought refuge in Allah's words as a shield. In your daily struggles—whether with anxiety, temptation, or injustice—do you actually turn to the Qur'an (not just its meaning but its very words) as your first and ultimate refuge? If not, what prevents you from doing so?
?
The absurd consequence of the metaphorical view is that contradictory commands would mean the same thing. If you treat parts of Allah's speech as less significant than others—skipping over verses that challenge your lifestyle—are you not, in practice, reducing the Qur'an to a single comfortable meaning? Will you commit today to reading the Qur'an with the awe it deserves, knowing each verse is distinct and uncreated?

The Mushaf is Truly Allah's Speech
Refutation of Mushaf-as-representation view with Tahawi's explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Salaf affirmed that the written Qur'an is truly Allah's speech and what the phrase 'minhu bada'a wa ilayhi ya'ud' means.

Core Idea
The Mushaf is not a representation; it is the very speech of Allah.

In this passage, the author addresses a serious theological error: the claim that the Qur'anic copies are only phrases or stories of Allah's speech, not His actual speech. He cites at-Tahawi and the Salaf to reaffirm that the Qur'an originated from Allah without modality and will one day return to Him. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its overall argument. Then read the translation. Notice the strong opening declaration and the subsequent explanation.
2
Identify the error being refuted
The author rejects the view that the Mushaf contains only a 'phrase' or 'story' of Allah's speech. Understand what the opponents claim and why it is problematic.
The opponents are likely influenced by Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila thought.
3
Analyze 'minhu bada'a' (from Him it began)
The Salaf used this phrase to counter the claim that Allah created speech in a locus. Focus on how the passage explains that Allah is the actual speaker, not a created thing.
The verse 41:2 is quoted as support.
4
Understand 'wa ilayhi ya'ud' (and to Him it returns)
This refers to the eschatological raising of the Qur'an from hearts and copies at the end of time, as mentioned in hadith.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how this passage defends the uncreatedness of the Qur'an while affirming its recitation and writing as truly His speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال: إن المكتوب في المصاحف عبارة عن كلام الله أو حكاية كلام الله وليس كلام الله، فقد خالف الكتاب والسنة وسلف الأمة. وكلام الطحاوي يردّ قول من قال : إنه معنى واحد لا يتصور سماعه منه، وأن المسموع المنزل المقروء المكتوب ليس بكلام الله، وإنما هو عبارة عنه، فإن الطحاوي يقول: كلام الله منه بدأ بلا كيفية أي لا نعرف كيفية تكلمه به وكذا قال غيره من السلف: «منه بدأ وإليه يعود». وإنما قالوا منه بدأ؛ لأن الجهمية من المعتزلة وغيرهم كانوا يقولون: إنه خلق الكلام في محل فقدر الكلام في ذلك المحل ، فقال السلف منه بدأ، أي هو المتكلم به فمنه بدأ، أي من بعض المخلوقات كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿تَنْزِيلٌ مِنَ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ﴾ [فصلت: ٢]. ومعنى قولهم: «وإليه يعود»، أنه يرفع من الصدور والمصاحف كما ورد في الأحاديث. انتهى.
And whoever says that what is written in the masahif (copies of the Qur'an) is a phrase for the speech of Allah or a story of the speech of Allah and is not the speech of Allah, has contradicted the Book, the Sunnah, and the predecessors of the community. And the speech of at-Tahawi refutes the saying of those who said: it is one meaning that cannot be conceived to be heard from Him, and that what is heard, revealed, recited, written is not the speech of Allah, but rather it is a phrase about it. For at-Tahawi says: The speech of Allah—from Him it began without modality, that is, we do not know the modality of His speaking it. And likewise, others from the predecessors said: 'From Him it began and to Him it returns.' They only said 'from Him it began' because the Jahmiyya from the Mu'tazila and others used to say: He created speech in a locus, and the speech came to be in that locus. So the predecessors said: 'From Him it began,' meaning He is the speaker with it, so from Him it began, not from some creatures, as Allah Almighty said: {A revelation from the Most Merciful, the Most Compassionate} [Fussilat: 2]. And the meaning of their saying: 'and to Him it returns' is that it will be raised from the hearts and masahif as has come in the hadiths. End.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine that the written Qur'an is not actually Allah's speech but only a phrase or a narrative. The author begins by declaring that such a view contradicts the Quran, Sunnah, and the consensus of the Salaf. Then he cites at-Tahawi's creed, which refutes those who say the speech is one meaning that cannot be heard and that the recited/written form is not Allah's speech. Tahawi affirms that the speech of Allah 'from Him it began' (minhu bada'a) without modality—meaning we do not know how He spoke it. The Salaf used this phrase to counter the Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila who claimed that Allah created speech in a created locus (like the air or a tree). By saying 'from Him it began,' they affirmed that Allah is the actual speaker, not that the speech originated from a created thing. This is supported by the Quranic verse: 'A revelation from the Most Merciful.' The second part of the phrase—'and to Him it returns' (wa ilayhi ya'ud)—is explained as referring to the eschatological event when the Qur'an will be raised from the hearts and written copies at the end of time, as mentioned in hadith. The passage ends with 'intaha' indicating the end of the quotation from Tahawi.

Key Terms
عبارة عن كلام الله
حكاية كلام الله
منه بدأ وإليه يعود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the written Qur'an (Mushaf) is only a representation or story of Allah's speech, not His actual speech.
Correction: The Salaf affirmed that the Mushaf contains the very speech of Allah, not a phrase or narrative about it.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'minhu bada'a' (from Him it began) according to the passage?
A) The Quran was created in a place.
B) Allah is the speaker, and the speech did not originate from a creature.
C) The speech came from the angel Jibril.
D) The Quran is eternal in its letters.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an in the Mushaf is truly the speech of Allah, not a creation or expression; it began from Him and will return to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly feel that you are hearing Allah's very speech, spoken directly by Him? If not, what would change in your recitation if you became fully convinced of this truth starting today? Make a concrete intention to recite even one verse with this awareness.
?
The Salaf said 'minhu bada'a' to defend the reality that Allah is the actual speaker. Do you ever speak about Allah as if He is distant or passive? How can you cultivate a deeper reverence for His attribute of speech in your daily prayers? Will you commit to pausing after reciting the Fatimah and reminding yourself that Allah is speaking to you through it?
?
The Qur'an will one day be raised from the hearts and masahif. If it were raised from your heart today, would it find a pure abode? What is one specific action you can take this week to ensure the Qur'an is truly alive in your heart, not just on a shelf or in your memory?

Interpreting 'To Him It Returns' and the Eternity of Attributes
Commentator interprets 'to Him it returns' and affirms attributes eternal, unlike creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's interpretation of the phrase 'wa ilayhi ya'ud' and the eternal, incomparable nature of all divine attributes.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are eternal and unlike creatures, even when named similarly.

In this passage, the commentator offers his own understanding of a key phrase from the creed, clarifying that it refers to the detailed knowledge of Allah's speech, not its removal. He then affirms that all divine attributes are eternal and utterly distinct from created attributes, even where names are shared.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without interpreting. Then read the translation. Notice the two distinct topics: the phrase 'wa ilayhi ya'ud' and the attributes.
The commentary is the author's own preferred view.
2
Identify the two main claims
First, the commentator explains what 'returns' means. Second, he affirms that all attributes are eternal and unlike creatures. Pinpoint where each claim begins.
Look for the pivot: 'wa sifatuhu...' (And His attributes...).
3
Examine the key terms
Focus on 'ilm tafsili kayfiyyah kalamihi' (detailed knowledge of modality of speech) and 'kunn haqiqa' (reality of essence). Also note 'ishtirak ismi' (nominal commonality).
These terms capture the commentator's careful nuance.
4
Understand the attribute distinction
The passage says attributes of Allah are eternal (fi al-azal) and do not resemble attributes of creatures. Even if names are the same, the realities differ. Ask yourself why this matters for theology.
This guards against anthropomorphism.
5
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question. It focuses on the commentator's interpretation of 'wa ilayhi ya'ud'.
Use only the passage to choose the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأظهر عندي أن «وإليه يعود»، يرجع إليه علم تفصيل كيفية كلامه وكنه حقيقة لا معنى مرامه، فإن سمع موسى كلامه لا يتصوّر أن يقال: سمعه كله أو بعضه. (وصفاته وفي نسخة: «لم يزل صفاته» (كلّها) أي ونعوت الباري جميعها واقعة في الأزل بخلاف صفات المخلوقين) أي لا تشابه نعوتهم وإن وقع الاشتراك الاسمي في صفات الحق
And the most apparent to me is that 'and to Him it returns' refers to the return of the detailed knowledge of the modality of His speech and the reality of its essence, not the meaning of its intent. For indeed, when Moses heard His speech, it is inconceivable that it be said he heard all of it or some of it. (And His attributes—in a version: 'His attributes never ceased'—all of them) meaning all of the attributes of the Creator are occurring in eternity, unlike the attributes of creatures. That is, they do not resemble their attributes, even if there is a nominal commonality in the attributes of the Truth.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage contains the commentator's own preferred understanding of the phrase 'wa ilayhi ya'ud' (and to Him it returns). He interprets it not as the literal removal of the text from existence, but rather that the detailed knowledge (ilm tafsili) of the modality (kayfiyya) and the true reality (kunn haqiqa) of Allah's speech will be restored to Him—meaning, only Allah fully comprehends its true nature. He supports this by noting that when Moses heard Allah's speech, it is impossible to say he heard all of it or even a part of it; the speech is beyond such distinctions. Then the commentator transitions to a note on the attributes of Allah (sifat) in general. He clarifies a textual variant: 'lam yazal sifatuhu' (His attributes never ceased) all of them. The meaning is that all of Allah's attributes are eternal (waqi'a fi al-azal), unlike the attributes of creatures. Even where there is a shared name (ishthirak ismi) between divine and human attributes (e.g., 'knowing', 'powerful'), the divine attributes are not comparable; they are transcendent and unique.

Key Terms
علم تفصيل كيفية كلامه
كنه حقيقة
وصفاته
الاشتراك الاسمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because the Qur'an mentions Allah's attributes like 'knowledge' and 'power', they are similar to human attributes in essence.
Correction: This passage clarifies that while there is nominal commonality (al-ishtirak al-ismi), the divine attributes are eternal and completely unlike created attributes; they are transcendent.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator understand by 'wa ilayhi ya'ud'?
A) The written copies will be raised.
B) The detailed knowledge of how Allah speaks returns to Him.
C) The speech ceases to exist.
D) The speech becomes eternal.

Key Takeaway
The commentator interprets 'to Him it returns' as referring to divine knowledge, not removal, and asserts all attributes are eternal and incomparable to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the names of Allah, do you ever internally imagine them as superhuman versions of your own attributes? The commentator insists that even the shared name implies no resemblance. What would it cost you to truly believe that—and what would it free you from?
?
Moses, the kalīm of Allah, could not fully grasp even a part of the speech he heard. You read about divine attributes in this lesson. Are you willing to admit that you understand almost nothing of their reality? If that humbles you, what should actually change in your worship today?
?
The attributes of Allah are eternal; yours are temporal. How does that difference shape the intimacy you feel with Him? Can you love a God whose attributes are not just greater versions of your own, but entirely other? Respond honestly: does that idea repel you or draw you nearer?

Divine Speech and Its Expressions
The author asserts that expressions of divine speech are created, but the speech itself is uncreated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine speech (kalam Allah) and its created expressions, and why the expressions are called God's speech only as indicators.

Core Idea
Expressions of God's speech are created, but His speech itself is uncreated and eternal.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial distinction in Islamic theology: the expressions (words, sounds) we use to convey God's speech are created, but the divine speech itself is uncreated. This distinction safeguards the attribute of speech from being likened to creatures while acknowledging the created nature of our recitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the author's central claim that expressions are created but the speech is uncreated.
Notice the phrase 'fi al-dalalat la fi al-madlul'.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between indicators (dalalat) and the indicated (madlul). Expressions are indicators and are created; the indicated (divine speech) is uncreated.
This distinction is foundational.
3
Understand the linguistic examples
The author gives examples: Arabic for Quran, Hebrew for Torah. This shows the speech is one, expressions vary across languages.
The different names do not imply different speeches.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this distinction is important to maintain that the Quran is uncreated while our recitation is created.
This safeguards divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصفاته كلّها بخلاف صفات المخلوقين، في الدلالات لا في المدلول وهذه العبارات مخلوقة، وسميت كلام الله لدلالتها عليه وتأديته، فإن عبّر بالعربية فهو قرآن، وإن عبّر بالعبرانية فهو توراة فاختلفت العبارات لا الكلام، قالوا وتسمى هذه العبارات كلام الله مجازا، وهذا كلام فاسد، فإن لازمه أن معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْرَبُوا الزِّنَا﴾ هو معنى قوله: ﴿وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ﴾، ومعنى آية الكرسي هو معنى آية المداينة، ومعنى سورة الإخلاص هو معنى سورة تبت يدا، ثم قال: تَبَّتْ ومن قال: إن المكتوب في المصاحف عبارة عن كلام الله أو حكاية كلام الله وليس كلام الله، فقد خالف الكتاب والسنة وسلف الأمة.
And all His attributes are unlike the attributes of creatures. In indicators (ad-dalalat) not in the indicated (al-madlul). And these expressions are created, and they are called the speech of God because they indicate it and convey it. For if it is expressed in Arabic, it is the Qur'an; and if it is expressed in Hebrew, it is the Torah. So the expressions differ, not the speech. They said: These expressions are called the speech of God metaphorically. And this is corrupt speech, for its necessary consequence is that the meaning of His statement 'Do not approach zina' is the same as the meaning of His statement 'Establish prayer', and the meaning of the Throne Verse is the same as the meaning of the Debt Verse, and the meaning of Surat al-Ikhlas is the same as the meaning of Surat Tabat Yada. Then he said: 'Tabat' (Perish). And whoever says that what is written in the codices is a phrase of God's speech or a narration of God's speech and not God's speech, has contradicted the Book, the Sunnah, and the Salaf of the Ummah.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine speech is uncreated and that the written Quran is truly God's speech. The author begins by stating that all of God's attributes are unlike those of creatures. Then he specifies that the expressions (al-‘ibarāt) used for God's speech are created, but they are called God's speech because they indicate His eternal speech. The expressions differ according to language—Arabic for the Quran, Hebrew for the Torah—but the speech itself is one. Those who claim that calling these expressions 'God's speech' is merely metaphorical are refuted. The author demonstrates the absurdity of this view: if the meaning of all scriptural commands is the same, then contradictory commands would have the same meaning, which is false. For example, the command against zina and the command to pray have different meanings. Therefore, the view that the Quran is just a phrase leads to nonsense. The passage concludes by declaring that anyone who denies that the written Quran is truly God's speech has contradicted the primary sources of Islam and the consensus of the Salaf.

كلام الله
عبارات
مخلوق
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some think that because the expressions are created, the Quran itself is created.
Correction: The passage distinguishes expressions from the divine speech itself. The expressions are created indicators, but the speech they indicate is uncreated.

Checkpoint

B) They are created.

Key Takeaway
Expressions of divine speech are created, but the divine speech itself is uncreated and one.

Refuting the Metaphorical View of the Quran
The author refutes the metaphorical interpretation of 'God's speech' and declares that the written Quran is truly His speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is erroneous to say the written Quran is only a metaphor for God's speech, and how this view leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
If the Quran were just a metaphorical phrase, contradictory commands would have the same meaning, which is false.

In this part of the passage, the author addresses a specific misinterpretation: that calling the expressions 'God's speech' is merely metaphorical. He shows that this view leads to a logical absurdity—if all scriptural texts are just metaphors for the same thing, then all commands would have identical meanings, which is clearly not the case. Therefore, the expressions truly indicate distinct divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the author's argument against the metaphorical view. Notice how he uses examples from the Quran itself.
The author uses strong language: 'this is corrupt speech'.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
The author argues that if the Quran is just a phrase, then different verses like 'Do not approach zina' and 'Establish prayer' would have the same meaning. Why is that absurd?
These verses have clearly different commands.
3
Consider the conclusion
The author ends by warning that denying the written Quran as true speech contradicts the Quran, Sunnah, and Salaf.
This shows the seriousness of the issue.
4
Reflect on the importance
Think about why this doctrine is critical for the Islamic belief in the Quran as the literal word of God.
It preserves the integrity of divine revelation.

وصفاته كلّها بخلاف صفات المخلوقين، في الدلالات لا في المدلول وهذه العبارات مخلوقة، وسميت كلام الله لدلالتها عليه وتأديته، فإن عبّر بالعربية فهو قرآن، وإن عبّر بالعبرانية فهو توراة فاختلفت العبارات لا الكلام، قالوا وتسمى هذه العبارات كلام الله مجازا، وهذا كلام فاسد، فإن لازمه أن معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْرَبُوا الزِّنَا﴾ هو معنى قوله: ﴿وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ﴾، ومعنى آية الكرسي هو معنى آية المداينة، ومعنى سورة الإخلاص هو معنى سورة تبت يدا، ثم قال: تَبَّتْ ومن قال: إن المكتوب في المصاحف عبارة عن كلام الله أو حكاية كلام الله وليس كلام الله، فقد خالف الكتاب والسنة وسلف الأمة.
And all His attributes are unlike the attributes of creatures. In indicators (ad-dalalat) not in the indicated (al-madlul). And these expressions are created, and they are called the speech of God because they indicate it and convey it. For if it is expressed in Arabic, it is the Qur'an; and if it is expressed in Hebrew, it is the Torah. So the expressions differ, not the speech. They said: These expressions are called the speech of God metaphorically. And this is corrupt speech, for its necessary consequence is that the meaning of His statement 'Do not approach zina' is the same as the meaning of His statement 'Establish prayer', and the meaning of the Throne Verse is the same as the meaning of the Debt Verse, and the meaning of Surat al-Ikhlas is the same as the meaning of Surat Tabat Yada. Then he said: 'Tabat' (Perish). And whoever says that what is written in the codices is a phrase of God's speech or a narration of God's speech and not God's speech, has contradicted the Book, the Sunnah, and the Salaf of the Ummah.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine speech is uncreated and that the written Quran is truly God's speech. The author begins by stating that all of God's attributes are unlike those of creatures. Then he specifies that the expressions (al-‘ibarāt) used for God's speech are created, but they are called God's speech because they indicate His eternal speech. The expressions differ according to language—Arabic for the Quran, Hebrew for the Torah—but the speech itself is one. Those who claim that calling these expressions 'God's speech' is merely metaphorical are refuted. The author demonstrates the absurdity of this view: if the meaning of all scriptural commands is the same, then contradictory commands would have the same meaning, which is false. For example, the command against zina and the command to pray have different meanings. Therefore, the view that the Quran is just a phrase leads to nonsense. The passage concludes by declaring that anyone who denies that the written Quran is truly God's speech has contradicted the primary sources of Islam and the consensus of the Salaf.

مصحف

Misconception: Some might think that the Quran in the mus-haf is just a representation, not the actual speech.
Correction: The author refutes this and asserts that it is truly God's speech; denying it contradicts the Quran, Sunnah, and Salaf.

Checkpoint
What consequence does the author draw from the metaphorical view?
A) The expressions become uncreated.
B) All scriptural commands would have the same meaning.
C) The Quran would be in multiple languages.
D) The Torah would be invalid.

Denying that the written Quran is God's speech is a grave error that contradicts the Quran, Sunnah, and Salaf.

?
The author calls this error 'fāsid' (corrupt). Is there any area in your faith where you have accepted a comfortable but logically inconsistent view? Will you have the courage to let it go and submit to the truth, even if it is uncomfortable?

Al-Tahawi on Divine Speech: From Him It Began, to Him It Returns
Quotes al-Tahawi and explains the Salafi phrase in response to Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Salafi phrase 'from Him it began and to Him it returns' as a refutation of the Jahmiyya view on divine speech and its two interpretations.

Core Idea
God's speech originates from Him, and its full knowledge returns to Him.

Al-Tahawi's statement is a cornerstone of Sunni belief on the Quran's uncreatedness. This passage explains the famous phrase that the Salaf used to counter those who claimed God's speech was created in a separate entity. We'll see both the historical context and a deeper theological interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for flow, then read the English translation. Notice the dense theological terminology.
The passage contains a quote from al-Tahawi and the author's own analysis.
2
Identify the two conflicting views
In your own words, describe what the Jahmiyya and the Salaf each said about divine speech. The passage explains why the Salaf said 'from Him it began'.
The Jahmiyya claimed God created speech in a locus; the Salaf affirmed that speech originates from God directly.
3
Examine the two meanings of 'to Him it returns'
The passage gives two interpretations: one literal (raising from hearts and codices) and the author's preferred theological one (return of detailed knowledge of modality). Compare them.
The author's interpretation focuses on the incomprehensibility of God's speech as experienced by Prophet Musa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلام الطحاوي يردّ قول من قال : إنه معنى واحد لا يتصور سماعه منه، وأن المسموع المنزل المقروء المكتوب ليس بكلام الله، وإنما هو عبارة عنه، فإن الطحاوي يقول: كلام الله منه بدأ بلا كيفية أي لا نعرف كيفية تكلمه به وكذا قال غيره من السلف: «منه بدأ وإليه يعود». وإنما قالوا منه بدأ؛ لأن الجهمية من المعتزلة وغيرهم كانوا يقولون: إنه خلق الكلام في محل فقدر الكلام في ذلك المحل ، فقال السلف منه بدأ، أي هو المتكلم به فمنه بدأ، أي من بعض المخلوقات كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿تَنْزِيلٌ مِنَ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ﴾ [فصلت: ٢]. ومعنى قولهم: «وإليه يعود»، أنه يرفع من الصدور والمصاحف كما ورد في الأحاديث. انتهى. والأظهر عندي أن «وإليه يعود»، يرجع إليه علم تفصيل كيفية كلامه وكنه حقيقة لا معنى مرامه، فإن سمع موسى كلامه لا يتصوّر أن يقال: سمعه كله أو بعضه.
And the statement of al-Tahawi refutes the one who said that it is a single meaning which cannot be conceived to be heard from Him, and that what is heard, revealed, recited, and written is not God's speech, but rather a phrase about it. For al-Tahawi says: God's speech from Him began without modality, i.e., we do not know the modality of His speaking it. And likewise other Salaf said: 'From Him it began and to Him it returns.' They said 'from Him it began' because the Jahmiyya of the Mu'tazila and others used to say: He created speech in a locus, and the speech occurred in that locus. So the Salaf said: 'from Him it began,' i.e., He is the one who spoke it, so from Him it began, not from some creatures, as God said: 'A revelation from the Most Merciful, the Most Merciful.' And the meaning of their saying 'to Him it returns' is that it will be raised from the hearts and the codices as has come in the hadiths. End. And the more apparent to me is that 'to Him it returns' refers to the return of detailed knowledge of the modality of His speech and the exact essence of its reality, not the meaning of his intent, for Musa heard His speech; it cannot be said that he heard all of it or some of it.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the uncreatedness of divine speech and an explanation of the Salafi phrase 'from Him it began and to Him it returns.' The author quotes al-Tahawi to refute those who claim that the revealed Quran is not actually God's speech but a mere phrase. Al-Tahawi affirms that God's speech began from Him without modality (bila kayfiyya). The phrase 'from Him it began' was used by the Salaf to counter the Jahmiyya view that God created speech in a locus (e.g., in the preserved tablet or in Jibril). Instead, He is the Speaker, and the speech originates from Him directly. The phrase 'to Him it returns' is interpreted by some as the physical raising of the Quran from hearts and codices at the end of time, as in Hadith. However, the author gives his own preferred interpretation: it means that the detailed knowledge of the modality and true reality of God's speech returns to Him alone, because when Musa heard God's speech, it cannot be conceived that he heard all of it or only a part—indicating that the full comprehension of divine speech is exclusive to God.

جهمية
متعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech is created in a separate locus (like the Preserved Tablet) and is not directly from Him.
Correction: The Salaf taught that God's speech originates from Him directly, without modality, and is not created in any creature.

Checkpoint

B) The detailed knowledge of God's speech returns to Him alone.
C) The speech returns to being uncreated.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'from Him it began and to Him it returns' affirms that God's speech is uncreated and that its full comprehension belongs to God alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the Qur'an were to be raised from the hearts and codices tomorrow, would you be among those who have internalized its message, or would you be left with empty pages? What one change can you make today to ensure the Qur'an lives in your heart?

The Eternal Unlikeness of Divine Attributes
Noting a variant reading and affirming divine attributes' unlikeness to creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all of Allah's attributes are eternal and completely unlike those of creatures, even when the same names are used.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are eternal and utterly unlike creation, even in shared names.

This lesson examines a brief but important passage from the commentary that clarifies a variant reading of the matn. It reinforces the core Ash‘ari/Maturidi doctrine that Allah's attributes are preeternal and bear no resemblance to the attributes of created beings, addressing a potential confusion arising from the use of common names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the variant reading 'lam yazal sifatuhu' (His attributes have not ceased) and how the author comments on it.
The variant emphasizes the eternal, unceasing nature of God's attributes.
2
Understand the doctrine of preeternity
The author states that all of God's attributes occur 'fī al-azal' (in preeternity). Reflect on the contrast with created attributes that come into being in time.
This is a fundamental tenet of Sunni theology: attributes of Allah are eternal.
3
Grasp the concept of nominal sharing
Even though names like 'knowledge' or 'power' are used for both God and creatures (ishtirak ismi), the author insists there is no likeness in reality. Understand why this distinction matters for theological precision.
It guards against anthropomorphism while affirming the reality of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وصفاته وفي نسخة: «لم يزل صفاته» (كلّها ) أي ونعوت الباري جميعها واقعة في الأزل بخلاف صفات المخلوقين) أي لا تشابه نعوتهم وإن وقع الاشتراك الاسمي في صفات الحق
(And His attributes — and in one copy: 'His attributes have not ceased' — all of them, i.e., all the descriptions of the Existent One occur in preeternity, unlike the attributes of creatures, i.e., they do not resemble their descriptions even if there occurs a nominal sharing (al-ishtirak al-ismi) in the attributes of the Truth.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a textual note and clarification on the phrase 'His attributes' from the matn. The author mentions a variant reading: 'His attributes have not ceased' (lam yazal sifatuhu), emphasizing the eternal unceasing nature of God's attributes. He then explains that all attributes of God are preeternal (wāqi‘a fī al-azal) and are not like the attributes of creatures. Even if the same names are used for God and creatures, such as 'knowledge', 'power', 'will', the realities are completely different. This is a standard Ash‘ari/Maturidi emphasis on divine transcendence while affirming the names.

Correction: The author clarifies that any sharing is purely nominal; the divine attributes are absolutely unlike those of creatures in their reality.
